We need to cover all points with n lines. For k=2, we have 2 sunny lines. Could we have a construction that uses only 2 sunny lines and some non-sunny lines that cover all points? Perhaps we can use a different selection of non-sunny lines that leaves a set of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines. Let's think more systematically.

We want to cover all points in T. The total number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. We have n lines. Each line can be thought of as a set of points it contains from T. The covering condition is that the union of these sets is T.

We have exactly k sunny lines. The rest are non-sunny lines (from H,V,D). The non-sunny lines are limited in the directions they can cover. Perhaps we can use a combinatorial argument to bound k.

Observe that each non-sunny line (horizontal, vertical, or diagonal) is "axis-aligned" in the sense that its points have either constant y, constant x, or constant sum. These families are natural.

Let's denote the set of points in T. Consider the projection onto the x-axis: For each x from 1 to n, the points with that x are those with b from 1 to n+1-x. So the vertical lines x = a cover these columns. Similarly, horizontal lines y = b cover rows.

Now, if we have a line that is not sunny, it belongs to one of these three families. The sunny lines can be arbitrary lines with slope not 0, ∞, -1.

We need to find for which k there exists a covering with exactly k sunny lines.

From small n, k=0 works, k=1 works, k=3 works (for n=3,4,5). k=2 fails for n=3,4,5. Let's test n=6 quickly to see if k=2 becomes possible. n=6, triangle T: a,b≥1, a+b≤7. Points: (1,1)-(6,1):6 points; b=2: (1,2)-(5,2):5; b=3: (1,3)-(4,3):4; b=4: (1,4)-(3,4):3; b=5: (1,5)-(2,5):2; b=6: (1,6):1. Total N = 21. Need 6 lines.

We want to see if we can have exactly 2 sunny lines and 4 non-sunny lines covering all points. Could we use 4 horizontal lines y=1,2,3,4? That covers b=1:6 points; b=2:5; b=3:4; b=4:3; total 18 points. Uncovered: b=5:2 points; b=6:1 point; total 3 points. Again three points uncovered. Need 2 sunny lines to cover 3 points. As before, any two of these three points determine a non-sunny line. So two sunny lines cannot cover all three. Could we use a different set of 4 non-sunny lines? For instance, use 2 horizontal and 2 vertical? Let's try to cover as many points as possible with non-sunny lines, leaving a set that can be covered by 2 sunny lines. The maximum points per non-sunny line is 6 (horizontal y=1, vertical x=1, diagonal sum=7? sum=7: a from 1 to 6, b=7-a, so (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1) -> 6 points). So each non-sunny line can cover up to 6 points. With 4 non-sunny lines, we could potentially cover up to 24 points if disjoint, but we only have 21 points, so it's possible to cover all points if we can arrange them to be disjoint (no overlapping points). Is it possible to have 4 non-sunny lines (from H,V,D) that are pairwise disjoint in T and together cover all 21 points? That would require the lines to be chosen so that no point lies on two of them, and the union is T. Since we have 4 lines, and each covers at most 6 points, total distinct points covered ≤ 24, but we need exactly 21. So it's plausible. However, can we actually achieve that? Let's try to construct.

We have three families: H, V, D. Each line in a family is defined by a parameter (y for H, x for V, sum s for D). The sets of points they contain are: H_y: {(a,y): a=1..n+1-y}, V_x: {(x,b): b=1..n+1-x}, D_s: {(a,b): a+b=s, a,b≥1}. The triangle T has points with a+b ≤ n+1. The union of H_y and V_x will have all points with a=some x or b=some y. To be disjoint, we need that no point satisfies both conditions. That is, for any point (a,b) in T, it cannot be on both H_y and V_x if y=b and x=a? Actually if a point is on H_y, then b=y; on V_x, then a=x. So the intersection of H_y and V_x is (x,y) if x+y ≤ n+1. So to avoid intersection, we need that for any chosen horizontal line y=c and vertical line x=d, we have d+c > n+1. So if we choose both a horizontal and a vertical line, we must ensure their intercepts sum > n+1. That is possible, e.g., for n=6, take y=6 (covers (1,6) only), x=6 (covers (6,1) only), then 6+6=12 >7, so intersection (6,6) not in T because sum=12>7. So they are disjoint.

Similarly, for horizontal and diagonal: intersection of y=c and x+y=s gives (s-c,c). This point is in T if s-c ≥1 and c≥1 and s-c+c=s ≤ n+1. So to avoid intersection, we need either s-c <1 or s > n+1 (but s ≤ n+1 for diagonal covering points in T? Actually we can choose diagonal lines with s between 2 and n+1 to cover points; s > n+1 would have no points in T). So we need s-c <1 => c > s-1. Since c ≤ n, and s ≤ n+1, it's possible to choose c > s-1. For instance, for n=6, take y=6 and diagonal s=7? s=7 gives points (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1). Intersection with y=6: (1,6) which is in T (since 1+6=7≤7). So that's an overlap. To avoid, we could choose y=5 and s=7: intersection (2,5) sum=7, point in T? 2+5=7≤7, yes, so overlap. y=4 and s=7: (3,4) sum=7, point in T. y=3 and s=7: (4,3) sum=7, point in T. y=2 and s=7: (5,2) sum=7, point in T. y=1 and s=7: (6,1) sum=7, point in T. So any horizontal line with y between 1 and 6 will intersect the diagonal s=7 at a point in T? Actually for y=1, intersection (6,1) is in T because 6+1=7≤7. So all horizontal lines y=c with c≥1 intersect the diagonal s=7 at (7-c,c) provided 7-c ≥1 and c≥1. For c=1..6, 7-c≥1, so yes. So to avoid intersection, we would need to choose a diagonal line with s > n+1? Then no points in T, so not useful. Or choose a horizontal line with y > s-1? For s=7, y >6 means y≥7, but y max is 6, so impossible. So if we include the diagonal s=7 (the longest diagonal), it will intersect every horizontal line y=c with c from 1 to 6 at a point in T. So to have disjointness, we cannot have both a horizontal line and the diagonal s=7. Similarly, for vertical lines and diagonal: intersection of x=a and x+y=s gives (a, s-a). This point is in T if a≥1, s-a≥1, and s ≤ n+1. So for s=n+1, vertical lines x=a with a=1..n will intersect at (a, n+1-a) which is in T because a + (n+1-a)=n+1≤n+1. So again, if we include the diagonal s=n+1, it intersects every vertical line. So to have disjointness, we cannot include both a vertical line and the diagonal s=n+1. Similarly, for two diagonals: two different sums s1 and s2, they are disjoint because they have no points in common (since points have unique sum). So two diagonals are automatically disjoint.

Thus, to have a set of non-sunny lines that are pairwise disjoint in T, we must avoid combinations that cause overlap. The overlaps occur when:
- Horizontal y=c and vertical x=d: intersection (d,c) is in T iff d+c ≤ n+1.
- Horizontal y=c and diagonal x+y=s: intersection (s-c,c) is in T iff s-c ≥1 and c≥1 and s ≤ n+1 (but if s ≤ n+1, s-c could be <1? Actually if s-c <1, then intersection is not a point of T because x positive; so we need s-c ≥1). So overlap if s-c ≥1 and c≥1 and s ≤ n+1.
- Vertical x=a and diagonal x+y=s: intersection (a, s-a) is in T iff a≥1, s-a≥1, and s ≤ n+1.

So to avoid overlaps, we need to choose lines such that for any pair, the intersection point is not in T (i.e., either coordinates not positive or sum > n+1). Since we only consider lines that actually contain points in T (otherwise they are useless), we want lines that are "far apart". 

Now, we need to cover all points with n lines. For k=2, we have 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines must cover all points except possibly those covered by sunny lines. The total number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. Each sunny line can cover at most something; but we might use a construction where the non-sunny lines are chosen to cover all points except a small set, and the sunny lines cover that small set. For n=4, k=4 we already saw fails because we only have 4 lines, each sunny line can cover at most 2 points (since y=x covers (1,1),(2,2); y=2x covers (1,2); etc.), total max points covered ≤ 8, but we need 10 points, so impossible.

Thus k=4 seems impossible for n=4,5,6. What about k=5 for n=5? That would be 5 sunny lines and 0 non-sunny lines. With 5 lines, can we cover all 15 points? Each sunny line can cover at most 3 points (y=x covers 3 points; y=2x covers 2; etc.). So 5 lines could cover at most 15 points if each covers exactly 3 points and they are disjoint. But can we have 5 disjoint sunny lines each covering 3 points? The triangle has 15 points, so it's possible in principle. But we need to see if such a configuration exists. For n=5, we can try to use lines like y=x (covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3)); y=x+1 (covers (1,2),(2,3) but (3,4) not in T; so only 2 points); y=x+2 (covers (1,3),(2,4) but (3,5) not; 2 points); y=x+3 (covers (1,4),(2,5) but (3,6) not; 2 points); y=2x (covers (1,2) and (2,4) maybe? (3,6) not; 2 points). So we cannot get 5 lines each covering 3 points. The maximum points per sunny line is 3 for y=x, but that line covers 3 points; other lines cover at most 2. So with 5 lines, the maximum total points covered is 3 + 2*4 = 11, less than 15. So impossible. Thus k=5 fails.

So far, for n=3, possible k:0,1,3; for n=4:0,1,3; for n=5:0,1,3; for n=6:0,1,3? We need to test n=6 k=3. We already have a construction for n=6 k=3? Possibly similar to n=5 k=3 using y=1,2,3 as non-sunny lines and then sunny lines covering the remaining points? For n=6, take non-sunny lines y=1,2,3 (covers b=1:6, b=2:5, b=3:4 -> total 15 points). Uncovered: b=4:3 points (1,4),(2,4),(3,4); b=5:2 points (1,5),(2,5); b=6:1 point (1,6). That's 6 points. Need 3 sunny lines to cover these 6 points. That gives (1,4),(2,5) (3,6) not in T, so covers A and D? Actually (1,4) and (2,5) are on line y=x+3; also (3,6) is not in T because sum=9>7; so that line covers (1,4) and (2,5). That's 2 points. After using y=x+3 (covers A and D) and y=-2x+8 (covers B and F? Actually B is (2,4) covered by y=-2x+8; F is (1,6) covered by that line; so that line covers (2,4) and (1,6). So left: (3,4) and (1,5) and maybe (2,5) already covered. So uncovered: (3,4) and (1,5). Are they on a sunny line? (1,5) and (3,4): slope=(4-5)/(3-1)= -1/2, sunny. So that line covers them. So we have three sunny lines: y=x+3 (covers (1,4),(2,5)); y=-2x+8 (covers (2,4),(1,6)); y=-1/2 x + 9/2 (covers (3,4),(1,5)). That covers all 6 points. So k=3 works for n=6.

Thus it appears that for n≥3, k=0,1,3 are always achievable. We need to check if k=2 is ever possible. We have seen it fails for n=3,4,5,6. Could it work for larger n? Let's try to reason generally.

We need to cover all points in T. Suppose we have exactly k sunny lines. For k=2, we have 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are from H,V,D. The covering condition is that every point in T is on at least one of these lines.

Observation: The non-sunny lines are axis-aligned or slope -1. They are "directions" that are natural. Perhaps we can use a combinatorial argument about the number of points on each "level". For a given y, the points (a,y) are covered by a horizontal line y = constant. So if we have a horizontal line at y = c, it covers all points with that y. Similarly, vertical lines cover all points with a given x. Diagonal lines cover all points with a given sum.

Now, consider the set of points in T. They form a triangular array. We want to cover them with n lines. Since we have n lines total, and there are N = n(n+1)/2 points, on average each line covers about (n+1)/2 points. The sunny lines might cover fewer points because they are not "parallel" to the axes or x+y=0; they can only contain at most floor((n+1)/2) points? Actually let's compute the maximum number of integer points on a line with slope not 0,∞,-1 within T. For a line y = mx + c, with m rational, the integer points are of the form (x, mx+c). Since the triangle has bounded coordinates, the maximum number of points is limited. For m=1, the line y = x + b gives points (a, a+b) with a ≥ 1, a+b ≥ 1, a + (a+b) ≤ n+1 => 2a+b ≤ n+1. So a can go up to floor((n+1-b)/2). For b=0, a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So max points = floor((n+1)/2). For m=2, line y = 2x + c gives points (a, 2a+c) with sum a+2a+c=3a+c ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1-c)/3). For c=0, a ≤ floor((n+1)/3). So fewer. For m=-2, line y = -2x + c gives sum a + (-2a+c)=c-a ≤ n+1 => a ≥ c - (n+1). Also a≥1, so limited. For m=-1/2, similar. So the maximum points on a sunny line is at most floor((n+1)/2) (achieved by y=x). Actually y=x covers points (a,a) with a ≤ floor((n+1)/2) because sum=2a ≤ n+1. So at most floor((n+1)/2). For n=3, floor(4/2)=2; we found sunny lines covering 2 points. For n=4, floor(5/2)=2; we had sunny lines covering 2 points. For n=5, floor(6/2)=3; we had y=x covering 3 points. For n=6, floor(7/2)=3; we had y=x covering 3 points? Actually y=x: points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) for n=6 (since sum=6≤7), so 3 points. So max points per sunny line is about n/2. But note that in our constructions for k=3, we used sunny lines that covered at most 2 points each for n=4,5,6? For n=4, we used sunny lines covering 2 points; for n=5, we used sunny lines covering 2 points; for n=6, we used sunny lines covering 2 points? Actually we used y=x+3 covering 2 points; y=-2x+8 covering 2 points; y=-1/2 x+9/2 covering 2 points. So they covered 2 points each. So the maximum points per sunny line is not always attained; it depends on the arrangement.

Now, for k=2, we have 2 sunny lines. Their total coverage can be at most 2*floor((n+1)/2) ≈ n, but we have n-2 non-sunny lines that can cover many points. However, the non-sunny lines are limited in the sense that they can only cover points along rows, columns, or diagonals. Perhaps we can show that if there are exactly 2 sunny lines, then the total number of points covered by the non-sunny lines is at most something, leaving at least 3 points uncovered that cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines.

Alternatively, we can consider the following: For any line L (not sunny), its points in T have either constant y, constant x, or constant sum. The union of all such lines must cover T. Suppose we have n lines total, with exactly k sunny lines. The complementary set of points (those not on non-sunny lines) must be covered by sunny lines. If we have many non-sunny lines, they may cover most points. But we need to find a necessary condition on k.

Let's denote by R the set of rows (y-values) that have a horizontal line present. Similarly, C for columns (x-values), and D for diagonals (sums). Then the points covered by non-sunny lines are those with y in R, or x in C, or sum in D. The sunny lines cover the remaining points.

We need to cover all points. Consider the points with maximum x or y. For instance, point (n,1) (the bottom right corner) has sum = n+1. This point is covered by a horizontal line y=1 if we have that; by a vertical line x=n if we have that; by a diagonal sum=n+1 if we have that. Similarly, point (1,n) is covered by y=n, x=1, sum=n+1. So to cover these corners, we likely need at least one of these families.

Now, for k=2, we have 2 sunny lines. Could we choose non-sunny lines to cover all points except a set of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines? Let's try to see if it's possible for large n.

Now, we can try to prove the general statement: For any n≥3, the maximum number of points in T that can be covered by n-2 lines from H,V,D is N-3. This seems plausible because the three points (1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n) are the "top" points, and they are such that any non-sunny line that contains any of them must be from a specific family and cannot contain all three. But we need to show that the coverage is limited.

Alternatively, we can consider the dual: Each non-sunny line is either horizontal, vertical, or diagonal. The points in T have coordinates. Perhaps we can use a counting argument: For a given set of n-2 lines, the number of points they cover is at most N - (number of points that are not covered). We want to show that at least 3 points are not covered. Suppose we have a set S of n-2 lines. Consider the "row" y = n-1. The points in this row are (1,n-1),(2,n-1). These are in T. Perhaps we can prove that the three points (1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n) are always such that at least one of them is not covered by any line, and the union cannot cover more than N-3 points.

Maybe we can use a combinatorial argument based on the fact that the set of points in T can be partitioned into n "levels" by the sum? Not exactly.

Let's consider the projection onto the x-axis. Each vertical line x=a covers the points with that a. Each horizontal line y=b covers points with that b. Each diagonal line s covers points with that sum. The points in T are such that a+b ≤ n+1.

Now, suppose we have a set L of non-sunny lines. For each a, define the number of points with x=a that are covered by the lines. Similarly for y. But we need to count total coverage.

Idea: Count the number of points that are not covered by any line in L. Show that there are at least 3 such points. The three points we suspect are the ones with maximum b or sum. Perhaps we can use a "corner" argument: Consider the point (1,n). It is covered by a horizontal line y=n, a vertical line x=1, or a diagonal line s=n+1. If we have a line that covers it, we might still have other points uncovered.

We could try to prove that the minimum number of non-sunny lines needed to cover T is n. That is, you cannot cover all points with fewer than n lines from H,V,D. This is a known result: The triangular grid of points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1 can be covered by n lines, but not by n-1 lines. Let's test for n=3: T has 6 points; can we cover with 2 lines? Thus the minimum number of non-sunny lines needed to cover T is n. This is a plausible theorem.

Now, if the minimum number is n, then any set of n-2 lines cannot cover T. But could they cover all but 2 points? That would mean that the uncovered set size is 2. That would imply that there exists a set of n-2 lines that covers N-2 points. That does not contradict the minimum being n because the minimum is about covering all points, not covering most. But we need to prove that for any n≥3, the maximum union size from n-2 non-sunny lines is N-3. This is equivalent to saying that for any set of n-2 lines from H,V,D, there are at least 3 points of T not on any of them. Since we have a candidate set of 3 points: (1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n). We can try to show that these three points are always such that at least one is not covered. But more importantly, that the total number of uncovered points is at least 3. Perhaps we can use a combinatorial inequality: For each line, the number of points it covers is at most n (since the maximum size is n for H1, V1, D_{n+1}). The sum of sizes of n-2 lines is at most (n-2)n. However, we need to subtract overlaps. Overlaps occur when a point is on two lines. To get a union of size N, we would need the sum minus overlaps to be N. But N is about n^2/2. So if we have n-2 lines, the sum of sizes is about n^2, which is twice N. So to get a union of size N-2, the overlaps must be at least about n^2 - (N-2) ≈ n^2 - n^2/2 = n^2/2. That is a large number of overlaps. So it might be impossible to have such large overlaps because each line can only intersect others in a limited way. Let's try to bound the maximum possible union size from m lines.

We have three families. Lines from the same family are disjoint (since they have different parameters). So if we take a set of lines, we can consider how many from each family. Let h, v, d be the numbers of horizontals, verticals, diagonals chosen. Then h+v+d = m. The union size is at most the total number of points covered, which can be computed as:

- Horizontal lines: each horizontal line y=c covers points (a,c) for a=1..n+1-c. So the set of points covered by horizontals is the union of rows 1..n. If we take multiple horizontals, they are disjoint, so they cover exactly the sum of their row sizes. So contribution = sum_{c in H} (n+1-c). Similarly, verticals: sum_{a in V} (n+1-a). Diagonals: sum_{s in D} (s-1). But these contributions may overlap because a point can be on a horizontal and a vertical, or a horizontal and a diagonal, etc. The total union size = (sum of sizes) - (overlaps). Overlaps are points that lie on at least two lines.

To maximize union, we want to minimize overlaps. The overlaps occur when a point satisfies two conditions. For example, a point (a,b) is on a horizontal y=b and a vertical x=a, so it's an overlap between H and V. It is also on a horizontal y=b and a diagonal x+y=s if s=a+b. Similarly for vertical and diagonal. So to avoid overlaps, we would want to choose lines that are "far apart" so that their intersections lie outside T. But we have to cover many points; we cannot avoid overlaps entirely. The maximum union size from m lines is something like N - (something). We need to find the maximum possible union size for m=n-2.

Let's denote the set of points in T as a grid. Perhaps we can use the concept of "covering number" of a triangular grid by axis-aligned lines. Actually if you take n-1 rows, you miss one row, say y=k. The points in that row are (1,k) to (n+1-k,k). If we take n-1 horizontals, they cover N - (n+1-k) points? Actually if we take all horizontals except one, we cover N - (n+1-k) points (since the missing row has n+1-k points). So the minimum is n.

Now, we need to prove that for any n, the maximum number of points in T_n that can be covered by n-2 lines is N-3. This is equivalent to: For any set of n-2 lines from H,V,D, there exist at least 3 points of T_n not covered. Which 3? Possibly (1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n). But we need to show that these three are not all covered? Actually if we cover all three, we still have other points uncovered. But we need to show that at least 3 points are uncovered in total. So we need to show that the union cannot have size N-2 or N-1. So it's a bound.

One approach: Consider the "sum" of points covered by the lines. The total number of points in T_n is N. Each line can be thought of as a set of points. We can use a "charging" argument: For each point not covered, we assign it to some line that could have covered it but didn't. Or we can use the fact that the three points (1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n) are the only points that have b≥n-1. For any line L, if L covers any of these points, it must be from a specific family. Let's define the "top row" y=n-1 and y=n. The points in these rows are exactly the three points. Now, suppose we have a set L of m lines. The total number of points in T_n is N. The number of points covered by L is at most something like: The sum of sizes of the lines minus the number of intersections among them. The intersections occur when a point lies on two lines. To maximize coverage, we want to minimize intersections. However, we have to cover many points; the top region might force intersections.

Another angle: Use the concept of "covering number" of a triangular grid by lines. It is known that the minimum number of lines needed to cover all points of a triangular grid of side n is n. Moreover, the maximum number of points you can cover with n-1 lines is N-2? Actually for a grid of points (i,j) with i,j from 1 to n, the minimum number of lines to cover all points is n (by taking all rows). But with n-1 lines, you can cover n(n-1) points? Not exactly. For a square grid, the minimum number of axis-aligned lines to cover all points is n (rows). With n-1 rows, you cover n(n-1) points, leaving n points uncovered. So the maximum coverage with n-1 lines is n(n-1). That's N - n. For triangular grid, the pattern is similar but with the top corner.

We can attempt to prove by induction or by a combinatorial inequality.

Let L be a set of lines. For each line, define its "size" (number of points in T_n it contains). For horizontals: size = n+1-c. For verticals: size = n+1-a. For diagonals: size = s-1. Note that all these sizes are between 1 and n. The sum of sizes is at most (n-2)n. But we need to subtract overlaps. Overlaps occur when a point is on two lines. Let's count the number of overlapping points. Each pair of lines from different families can intersect at a point. Overlaps between same family are disjoint. So total overlaps = sum over pairs (L_i, L_j) of 1 if they intersect at a point in T_n. However, we need to consider that a point can be on more than two lines, so overlapping count is not simply the number of pairs; we need to use inclusion-exclusion. But for maximizing union, we want to minimize overlaps. So the maximum union is achieved when the lines are chosen to have as few intersections as possible. The intersections that are unavoidable might be those that involve the top points.

Perhaps we can show that any set of n-2 lines must have at least 3 intersections with each other (i.e., at least 3 points that are on two lines). But we need to show that the union size is at most N-3. Since N is the total points, if we have at least 3 points that are not covered, then union ≤ N-3. So we need to show that there are at least 3 points not covered by L.

Suppose L covers all but at most 2 points. Then the uncovered set has size 0,1,2. If it has size 0, then L covers T_n. But we know that the minimum number of lines to cover T_n is n, so with n-2 lines it's impossible to cover all points. So that case is ruled out. If it has size 1, then there is exactly one point not covered. Could that happen? For n=3, with 1 line, we can cover 3 points, leaving 3 uncovered. So not 1. For n=4, with 2 lines, we can cover 7 points, leaving 3 uncovered. So not 1. For n=5, with 3 lines, max coverage 12, leaving 3 uncovered. So not 1. For n=6, with 4 lines, max 18, leaving 3 uncovered. So it seems the uncovered set always has at least 3 points. So we need to prove that for any n, with m = n-2 lines, the number of uncovered points is at least 3.

One could attempt to prove that the three points (1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n) are always uncovered. Is that true? Not necessarily; we can have a line that covers them, e.g., horizontal y=n-1 covers (1,n-1),(2,n-1); but then (1,n) is uncovered. So at least one of them is uncovered. But we need at least 3 uncovered overall. Could it be that we cover all other points except these three? For n=5, with y=4 and x=1, we covered 6 points, leaving 9 uncovered. Not just the three. So the uncovered set is larger. For n=5, we need to show that any set of 3 lines leaves at least 3 points uncovered. That is equivalent to showing that the maximum union size is 12. We can try to prove a general bound: For any set of m lines, the union size ≤ N - (n - m) maybe? For m=n-2, that gives N - 2, but that's not matching. For m=n-2, N - (n - m) = N - 2, but we have N-3. So it's one less. So maybe the bound is: For any set of m lines, the union size ≤ N - (n - m) - 1? For m=n-2, that gives N - 2 - 1 = N-3. For m=n-1, that would give N - 1 - 1 = N-2, but we know with n-1 lines you can cover N-1 points (by taking all rows except one). So that bound is not valid.

Let's try to derive a general inequality. Consider the set of points in T_n. They can be partitioned into n "levels" by the sum of coordinates? For each s from 2 to n+1, the points with a+b=s form a diagonal. The number of points on diagonal s is s-1. The total points is sum_{s=2}^{n+1} (s-1) = n(n+1)/2. Now, each line in H covers exactly one point from each diagonal? No, horizontal line y=c covers points (a,c) with a=1..n+1-c; the sum a+c ranges from 1+c to n+1; so it covers points with various sums. So it's not a simple partition.

Perhaps we can use a "covering" argument: Each line can cover at most n points. With m lines, the total number of points covered (counting multiplicities) is at most m*n. However, points may be covered multiple times. To cover N points, we need the sum of cover counts to be at least N. But we have m lines, each covering at most n points, so total coverage sum ≤ m*n. For m=n-2, this gives total coverage sum ≤ n(n-2). But N = n(n+1)/2. So n(n-2) is less than N for n≥3? Compare n(n-2) = n^2 - 2n; N = (n^2 + n)/2. For n=3, n(n-2)=3, N=6; 3<6. So total coverage sum is at most 3, but we need to cover 6 points, impossible. That's why we need more lines. But that's a trivial bound: each line covers at most n points, so with m lines we can cover at most m*n points. For m=n-2, that's n^2-2n; for n=5, that's 15, which is > N=15? Actually n^2-2n = 25-10=15, which equals N. So the trivial bound says we could cover at most 15 points with 3 lines, but we observed that with 3 lines we can cover at most 12 points. So the trivial bound is not tight; we need to consider that lines from the same family are disjoint, so the sum of sizes is at most something. Actually for m=3, the sum of sizes is at most 6+5+4=15 if we take horizontals? But we can also take a vertical and a diagonal; but the maximum sum of sizes for 3 lines is achieved by taking the three lines with the largest sizes: H1 (6), V1 (6), D7 (6) sum=18. However, these three lines have overlaps, so the union is less. So the bound from sum of sizes is not useful.

We need a more precise bound on the union size. Perhaps we can use the following idea: For each point (a,b) in T, consider the line that is "parallel" to one of the three directions. The three families are all that are not sunny. If we have m lines, we can think of them as "intervals" in the dual space. Alternatively, we can consider the "extremal" points: the three points (1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n) are the only points that have b≥n-1. For any line L, if L contains any of these points, it must be from a specific family. Now, suppose we have m lines. Let's count the number of points that are covered by at least one line. Consider the "top row" y=n. The only point there is (1,n). If we have a line that covers it, that line could be H_n, V_1, or D_{n+1}. If we have a line that covers it, we use one line. The next row y=n-1 has points (1,n-1),(2,n-1). To cover these, we might need a horizontal line y=n-1, or a vertical line x=1, or a diagonal line s=n, or a diagonal line s=n+1. But note that if we use a horizontal line y=n-1, it covers both points, but then we have used a line that covers the top row? Actually y=n-1 does not cover (1,n). So then (1,n) remains uncovered. Similarly, if we use x=1, it covers (1,n-1) and (1,n), but leaves (2,n-1) uncovered. If we use diagonal s=n, it covers (1,n-1) only. If we use diagonal s=n+1, it covers (1,n) and (2,n-1). The total number of such points is N-3. Can we cover all of them with m-2 lines? That would require that the minimum number of lines needed to cover the lower triangle (b≤n-2) is at most m-2 = n-4. But the lower triangle is not exactly T_{n-2}; it is a larger set. However, note that the points with b≤n-2 form a "triangular" shape? Actually they are all points with a+b ≤ n+1 and b≤n-2. So any set of m lines that includes some lines covering the top points will have to use at least 2 lines for the top points, leaving m-2 lines for the lower triangle. But the lower triangle has N-3 points. To cover all of them with m-2 lines, we would need m-2 ≥ (some number). The minimum number of lines to cover the lower triangle might be something like n-2? Let's examine. For n=5, lower triangle (b≤3) has 12 points. Can we cover all 12 points with 2 lines? Possibly with two vertical lines? So not. With H1,H2,H3 plus maybe a vertical? That's 4 lines. So minimum is 4 lines = n-2. So it seems that the lower triangle (points with b≤n-2) also requires at least n-2 lines to cover completely. And that's achievable by taking all rows 1..n-2. So with m = n-2 lines, the maximum coverage is N-3, because if you use some lines to cover the top points, you have fewer lines for the lower triangle, so you cannot cover all lower points. However, you might not need to cover all lower points if you can cover some of them with the top lines as well. But the top lines are from H_n, V_1, D_{n+1} etc., and they cover some points in the lower triangle too. So maybe you can cover more points overall by using a mix. But we observed that the maximum is N-3, which is exactly the number of points in the lower triangle (rows 1..n-2). So no set of n-2 lines can cover any point in the top two rows without missing some lower points. In other words, if you have n-2 lines, the set of points covered by them is a subset of T_n that has at most N-3 points. And the construction with n-2 horizontals achieves exactly N-3 points. So we need to prove that for any selection of n-2 lines, |U(L)| ≤ N-3.

We can try to prove by considering the "complement" of U(L). Let's denote the set of points in T_n that are not covered by L. We want to show that this set has at least 3 points. Maybe we can use the following: For any set of lines L, define the "minimum coordinate" in the sense of the parameters. Let's denote the set of horizontals chosen: H_c with c in C. Now, we need to prove this in general. Let's attempt a proof by considering the "sum of b" of points covered. For each line L, define the total "b-sum" over its points. For a horizontal line y=c, the b-sum is c * (number of points) = c*(n+1-c). For a vertical line x=a, the b-sum is sum_{b=1}^{n+1-a} b = (n+1-a)(n+2-a)/2. For a diagonal line s=d, the b-sum is sum_{a=1}^{d-1} (d-a) = (d-1)d/2. So these are known.

Now, suppose we have a set L of m lines. The total b-sum over all points in T_n is S = sum_{b=1}^n b*(n+1-b) = sum_{b=1}^n (b(n+1) - b^2) = (n+1) sum b - sum b^2 = (n+1)*n(n+1)/2 - n(n+1)(2n+1)/6 = n(n+1)[(n+1)/2 - (2n+1)/6] = n(n+1)[ (3(n+1) - (2n+1))/6 ] = n(n+1)[(3n+3-2n-1)/6] = n(n+1)(n+2)/6. So S = n(n+1)(n+2)/6.

Now, the b-sum over the union U(L) is at most the sum of b-sums over the lines, but we need to subtract overlaps. However, if a point is covered by multiple lines, it will be counted multiple times in the sum of b-sums. To get the total b-sum over U(L) (i.e., sum of b over points that are in U(L)), we have: Sum_{points in U(L)} b = (Sum_{L∈L} b-sum(L)) - (overlap contributions). The overlap contributions are points that are on two lines, counted twice, so they are subtracted once when we take the union sum. But we don't know the overlaps. However, we can get a lower bound on the sum of b-sums of the lines if the union is large. Since each line's b-sum is positive, the sum of b-sums is at least something. If we want to cover many points, we need the total b-sum of the lines to be at least the sum of b over those points. But we have m lines; we can try to maximize the union size given a fixed total b-sum of lines? Not directly.

Maybe we can use a "potential" function: For each point (a,b), assign a "charge" of 1. Then we can distribute the charge to the lines in a way that each line can cover at most something, and the total charge that can be covered is at most something. For instance, we can use the following: For any line L, the number of points it covers is at most n. But more importantly, if we consider the set of points in T_n that are not on any line in L, we can show that there are at least 3 such points by analyzing the "top" region.

Let's try a different approach: Use the concept of "covering" by lines of a right triangle. The three families are all lines that are not sunny. The set of all non-sunny lines is infinite; we can choose any. For a given selection of m lines, we can look at the "dual" as a set of points in a 3D grid. Another idea: For each line, consider its "intercept" in the coordinate plane. The points on a line L are those (x,y) such that some linear equation holds. For a horizontal line, y = c; for vertical, x = a; for diagonal, x+y = s. The condition for a point (x,y) to be in T_n is x≥1, y≥1, x+y≤n+1. So the set of points covered by a collection of such lines is the set of integer points in T_n that satisfy at least one of these linear equations. Now, we want to bound the number of integer points in T_n that are not in the union. This is like the complement of a set of lines. Perhaps we can use a "coloring" argument: Color each point with the sum of its coordinates modulo something. For the lines we consider, they have either constant x, constant y, or constant x+y. The points with constant x+y are on diagonals. The three families cover all points that are on lines of these types. The points that are not on any of these lines are those that avoid all these linear equations. In a triangle, it's known that the maximum number of points you can cover with m lines from these three families is N - (n - m) for m ≤ n? Actually for a square grid of side n, you can cover all points with n lines (rows). With n-1 lines, you cover n(n-1) points, leaving n points uncovered. So the uncovered count is n. For triangular grid, the uncovered count is 3 when using n-2 lines? That is, with n-2 lines you can cover all but 3 points. That seems plausible.

Let's compute the maximum coverage for m lines. For m lines, the best is to take the m rows with the largest sizes: H1, H2, ..., Hm. Their sizes are n+1-1=n, n+1-2=n-1, ..., n+1-m. So union size = sum_{i=1}^m (n+1-i) = m(n+1) - m(m+1)/2. That's a candidate. For m=n-2, that's (n-2)(n+1) - (n-2)(n-1)/2 = (n-2)[(n+1) - (n-1)/2] = (n-2)[(2n+2 - n+1)/2] = (n-2)(n+3)/2 = N - 3? Perhaps we can use the concept of "covering number" in a bipartite graph. The points in T can be thought of as edges in a complete bipartite graph between two sets of size n? Not.

Another approach: For each point (a,b) in T, consider its "distance" from the origin. The lines we consider are of the form x = a, y = b, x+y = s. These are all lines that are either vertical, horizontal, or diagonal. The union of such lines can be seen as the set of points that satisfy at least one of these equations. The complement consists of points that avoid all these equations. In a triangle, there is a known result that the maximum number of points that can be covered by k such lines is N - (n - k) for k ≤ n? Actually for a square grid of side n, the minimum number of lines (rows and columns) to cover all points is n, and with n-1 lines you can cover n(n-1) points, leaving n points uncovered. So the uncovered count is n. For triangular grid, it's similar but with the diagonal family.

We can try to prove by induction on n: For n, the maximum number of points that can be covered by m lines from H,V,D is N - (n - m) for m ≤ n? Let's test with n=5, m=3: N=15, n-m=2, N-2=13, but actual max from 3 lines is 12. So that fails. Maybe it's N - (n - m) - 1 for m ≤ n-2? For m=3, n=5, N-(2)-1=15-2-1=12, matches. For m=4, n=6, N-(2)-1=21-2-1=18, matches rows? Actually for n=6, m=4, rows give 6+5+4+3=18, yes. So for m=n-2, formula gives N-3. For m=n-3, formula gives N-4? For n=6, m=3, N-4=17, but actual max is 15. So that's not consistent. So maybe the formula depends on the specific m.

D7: (1,6)-(6,1); (6,1) on H1; (5,2) on H2? etc. So 6 overlaps? Might give 22. So likely rows are best. So for m=4, max is 26. Then uncovered =10 points. So insufficient. Thus k=4 fails for n=8 as well.

So it seems that for k≥2, the number of sunny lines is at least 3 to cover the uncovered points. More generally, if we have m = n - k non-sunny lines, the maximum coverage from these lines is something like: The best is to take the m rows (or columns) that cover the most points. The number of points covered by m rows is S_m = sum_{i=1}^m (n+1-i) = m(n+1) - m(m+1)/2. For m = n - k, this is (n-k)(n+1) - (n-k)(n-k+1)/2 = (n-k)[(n+1) - (n-k+1)/2] = (n-k)[(2n+2 - n + k -1)/2] = (n-k)(n+k+1)/2. That's N - (k(n+1) - k^2)/2? Actually N = n(n+1)/2. So S_m = N - (k(n+1) - k^2)/2 = N - (k(n+1) - k^2)/2. For k=2, S_m = N - (2(n+1)-4)/2 = N - (2n+2-4)/2 = N - (2n-2)/2 = N - (n-1). For n=7, that's 28-6=22, but we actually found a set of 5 lines (n-2=5) covering 25, not 22. So the rows are not the best; a mix can do better. So the maximum coverage is not simply rows. For n=7, m=5, rows give 7+6+5+4+3=25, which matches the best we found (H1-H4+D8 gave 25). So for m=5, rows are best. For m=4, rows give 7+6+5+4=22, but we found a set (H1,H2,H3,D8) giving 25, which is better. So the maximum coverage is not monotonic in m? Actually for m=4, we got 25; for m=5, we got 25; so the maximum coverage jumps. So the best coverage with m lines can be more than the sum of the m largest rows. So we need to find the maximum possible union size for a given m. This is a known combinatorial geometry problem: The maximum number of lattice points in a right triangle that can be covered by m lines of the form x = a, y = b, or x+y = s. This might be related to the concept of "covering number" of a triangular grid by lines.

Thus for n=8, only k=0,1,3 work. For n=7, we have k=4 as well. So the set of possible k depends on n. We need to find all k for which there exists a covering with exactly k sunny lines.

Let's analyze more systematically. Let T_n be the triangular grid of points. Let H, V, D be the three families. We want to choose n lines: h horizontals, v verticals, d diagonals (with h+v+d = n - k) and k sunny lines (which are not from H,V,D). The covering condition: Every point (a,b) in T_n is on at least one of the chosen lines.

We can think of the non-sunny lines as a set of lines from H,V,D that cover some points. The sunny lines are arbitrary lines with slopes not 0,∞,-1. They can be any lines. So the problem reduces to: For a given n, what are the possible numbers of sunny lines k for which we can cover T_n with n lines, of which exactly k are sunny? This is a combinatorial covering problem.

One approach: Consider the "extremal" points of T_n. The point (1,1) is the bottom-left corner. It is covered by H_1 (y=1), V_1 (x=1), D_2 (x+y=2). Similarly, the point (1,n) (top-left) is covered by H_n (y=n), V_1 (x=1), D_{n+1} (x+y=n+1). The point (n,1) (bottom-right) is covered by H_1 (y=1), V_n (x=n), D_{n+1} (x+y=n+1). So these corner points are natural.

We can use a "potential" function: For each line, define its "cost" as something. Perhaps we can derive a necessary condition on k by double counting the number of points covered by the lines. Another idea: Consider the sum over all points (a,b) of something like 1/(a+b). Not.

The points with b=n-1 are covered by y=n-1. So all points covered. So k=1 works.

Similarly we could take n-1 vertical lines and one sunny line through (n,1). Or n-1 diagonals x+y=c (c=2,...,n) and one sunny line through (1,n) or (n,1) etc. But careful: If we take diagonals x+y=c for c=2,...,n (n-1 lines), they cover all points with sum from 2 to n. The points with sum n+1 are those with a+b=n+1, a,b≥1. Those are points (1,n), (2,n-1), ..., (n,1). So we need to cover those. Could we cover all these with one sunny line? Possibly if the sunny line passes through all these points? That would be a line containing n points, but these points lie on the line x+y=n+1 (a diagonal) which is parallel to x+y=0? Actually the set of points with a+b=n+1 is a line (if we consider continuous line) but the line x+y=n+1 is a line with slope -1, which is not sunny. So we cannot use that line. But we could use a sunny line that passes through all these points? However, these points are not collinear except on the diagonal line x+y=n+1, which is slope -1. So they are not collinear on any other line. So one line cannot cover all points with sum n+1 because they are on a line of slope -1, but that line is not sunny. Could we cover them with multiple lines? But we have only one sunny line, and the rest are diagonals covering sums 2..n. So we need to cover points with sum n+1 using the sunny line. But the sunny line can cover at most some of them; we need to cover all points with sum n+1. So we need to see if we can choose a sunny line that passes through all points (a, n+1-a) for a=1..n. That would require them to be collinear on a line with slope not -1, which is impossible because they are already collinear on the line x+y=n+1 (slope -1) and any other line can intersect that line at most once. So one line cannot cover more than one point from that set. Therefore with n-1 diagonals covering sums 2..n, we cannot cover the n points on sum n+1 with one sunny line. So that configuration fails.

But maybe we can choose a different combination of non-sunny lines, not all from one family. For k=1, we have n-1 non-sunny lines. They could be a mix of horizontal, vertical, and diagonal lines. Could we cover all points with a mix of these? We need to cover all points in the triangular region. Since we have many lines, perhaps we can choose a set of n-1 lines that covers all points except a few, and then a sunny line covers the rest. However, we need exactly n lines total. The non-sunny lines are at most 3 families; we could use multiple lines from each family. The total number of lines is n, and we need to cover all points. It might be possible to have k=1 with a different selection.

But we already have a construction for k=1: n-1 horizontal lines and one sunny line through (1,n). That works. So k=1 is achievable.

Now k=2: need exactly 2 sunny lines, n-2 non-sunny lines. Can we construct? Possibly use n-2 horizontal lines and 2 sunny lines covering the remaining points? But if we use n-2 horizontal lines y=1,...,n-2, then points with b=n-1 and b=n are uncovered. Points with b=n-1: a from 1 to 2 (since a+b≤n+1 => a≤2). So points (1,n-1) and (2,n-1). Points with b=n: (1,n). So we have three points. Can two sunny lines cover all three? Possibly if each sunny line passes through some points. But we need to cover all points. Could we choose sunny lines such that together they cover these three points? For instance, one sunny line through (1,n) and (2,n-1)? That line would have slope? (n-1 - n)/(2-1) = -1/1 = -1, that's slope -1, not sunny. So that line is not sunny. Another: through (1,n) and (1,n-1) is vertical x=1, not sunny. Through (2,n-1) and (1,n-1) is horizontal y=n-1, not sunny. So any line through two of these points is either vertical, horizontal, or slope -1. So to cover these three points with two sunny lines, we would need each sunny line to cover at most one of these points? But then we have three points and only two lines, so one line would need to cover two points, but that line would be non-sunny. So it seems impossible to cover these three points with two sunny lines, because any two of them determine a line that is non-sunny. But we could also cover them using the non-sunny lines? But we already have only n-2 horizontal lines, which cover b=1..n-2. So the points with b=n-1 and n are uncovered. We could potentially use the non-sunny lines to cover some of them? For instance, we could include a vertical line x=1 or x=2, or a diagonal line x+y = something. But we have exactly n-2 non-sunny lines; we could choose them differently, not all horizontal. So we have flexibility: we can use any n-2 lines from the families of non-sunny lines (horizontal, vertical, diagonal). So we need to see if there exists a set of n-2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines that together cover all points.

We can try to design a construction for k=2. Perhaps we can use all n-1 horizontal lines? That would be n-1 lines, but we only have n-2 non-sunny lines, so we would have 1 sunny line? Actually if we use n-1 horizontal lines, that's n-1 lines, then we need 1 sunny line to get total n lines, but that gives k=1, not 2. So for k=2, we need 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. Possibly we can use n-2 horizontal lines, and then two sunny lines that cover the remaining points. But we saw that with n-2 horizontal lines, the uncovered points are those with b=n-1 and n. That's 3 points. Could two sunny lines cover these 3 points? They would need to cover all three, but any line through two of these points is non-sunny. So maybe we can use a sunny line that covers one point and another sunny line that covers the other two? But to cover two points, the line through them must be non-sunny (as argued). So that's not allowed. Could we cover the three points using the sunny lines plus possibly one of the non-sunny lines covering some? But the non-sunny lines we chose are horizontal lines y=1..n-2; they don't cover these points. So the only lines covering these points are the sunny lines. Since two sunny lines can cover at most two points if each line covers one point, but we have three points, so at least one point is uncovered. So that fails.

Maybe we can choose a different set of non-sunny lines, not all horizontal. For instance, we could use n-2 diagonal lines covering sums 2..n-1? Then points with sum n and n+1? Actually if we take diagonals x+y=c for c=2,...,n-1? That's n-2 lines. They cover points with sum from 2 to n-1. The uncovered points are those with sum n and n+1. How many points? For sum=n: a from 1 to n-1, b=n-a, so n-1 points. For sum=n+1: a from 1 to n, b=n+1-a, so n points. So total 2n-1 points. Two sunny lines cannot cover all that many points; they would cover at most 2*(some number) but also need to cover all these points. So not possible.

What about using a mix of horizontal and vertical lines? For instance, use n-2 horizontal lines covering b=1..n-2, and also include a vertical line x=1? But then we have total n-1 non-sunny lines? Wait: n-2 non-sunny lines total; we could use some horizontal and some vertical. For example, take n-3 horizontal lines y=1..n-3, and 1 vertical line x=1, and 1 diagonal? That would be n-2 non-sunny lines? Actually count: if we take n-3 horizontal lines, that's n-3 lines; plus 1 vertical = n-2 lines; that's exactly n-2 non-sunny lines. Then we have 2 sunny lines. The coverage: Horizontal lines cover b=1..n-3; vertical line x=1 covers points (1,b) for b=1..n? Actually points with x=1 and y from 1 to n? But need a+b≤n+1: For x=1, b can be from 1 to n (since 1+b ≤ n+1 => b≤n). So vertical line x=1 covers points (1,1) to (1,n). However, the horizontal lines y=1..n-3 cover those points with b=1..n-3 already; but vertical line x=1 covers (1,n-2), (1,n-1), (1,n) as well? Actually (1,n-2) is b=n-2, but horizontal line y=n-2 covers (1,n-2) and others; but vertical line x=1 also covers (1,n-2) but that's fine. So the points covered by non-sunny lines: all points with b=1..n-3 (from horizontal), and all points with x=1 (including those with b=1..n). But note that points with x=1 and b=1..n-3 are already covered. So the uncovered points are those with b≥n-2 and x>1? Let's list the triangular region: points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1. Non-sunny lines: y=1,...,n-3; x=1. So the uncovered points are those with b=n-2, n-1, n and a>1. Points with b=n-2: a from 2 to n-1? Actually a+b ≤ n+1 => a ≤ n+1-b. For b=n-2, a ≤ 3. So a=1,2,3. But x=1 covers a=1; horizontal lines y=n-2? Not covered because we only have y=1..n-3; so y=n-2 not present. So points (2,n-2) and (3,n-2) are uncovered. Similarly for b=n-1: a ≤ 2; points (1,n-1) covered by x=1; (2,n-1) uncovered. For b=n: a=1 only; (1,n) covered by x=1. So uncovered points: (2,n-2), (3,n-2), (2,n-1). That's 3 points. Can two sunny lines cover these three? Again, any two of these points determine a line: (2,n-2) and (3,n-2) are horizontal (y=n-2) not sunny; (2,n-2) and (2,n-1) vertical x=2 not sunny; (3,n-2) and (2,n-1) have slope? (n-1 - (n-2))/(2-3) = 1/(-1) = -1, so slope -1, not sunny. So any line through two of them is non-sunny. So two sunny lines cannot cover all three. Could we use a sunny line that covers one point and another sunny line that covers the other two? But to cover two points, the line must be non-sunny. So not possible.

Thus for k=2, it seems difficult to cover all points with n-2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines. But perhaps we can use more than 2 non-sunny lines from different families such that the uncovered points are fewer and can be covered by 2 sunny lines? We have total lines n, so we have n-2 non-sunny lines. We can choose any n-2 lines from the set of all lines that are not sunny (i.e., horizontal, vertical, or slope -1). We need to cover all points. Could we cover all points with exactly n lines, where n-2 are non-sunny and 2 sunny? That is equivalent to: there exists a set of n lines covering the triangle, with exactly 2 sunny lines. We need to determine if such a set exists for all n≥3, or maybe only for some n.

We should try to find a construction. Maybe we can use all n-1 horizontal lines? That gives k=1. For k=2, maybe we can use n-2 horizontal lines and 2 sunny lines that are both "steep" lines covering the missing rows? But the missing rows are two rows? Actually if we take horizontal lines y=1,...,n-2, we miss rows b=n-1 and n. That's two rows: row n-1 has points (1,n-1),(2,n-1) but (1,n-1) maybe covered? Actually horizontal lines y=1..n-2 cover b=1..n-2; so points with b=n-1 and b=n are uncovered. Row n-1: a=1,2 (since 1+b≤n+1 => a≤n+1-b=2). So points (1,n-1) and (2,n-1). Row n: a=1 only. So three points. Could two sunny lines cover these three? As argued, any line through two of these points is non-sunny, so the two sunny lines cannot cover all three because one line would need to cover two points, which would be non-sunny. But could we use a sunny line that covers one point and the other sunny line covers the other two? No, because covering two points forces that line to be non-sunny. So not possible.

What if we choose non-sunny lines not all horizontal? For example, use n-2 diagonal lines covering sums 2..n-1? That leaves many points. Not good.

Maybe we can use a combination that covers all points except a few that can be covered by two sunny lines. But we have only n-2 non-sunny lines; the total number of points is n(n+1)/2. The maximum coverage by n-2 non-sunny lines (each can cover many points) could be large. But we need to cover all points. Perhaps we can use lines that are "almost" covering the whole triangle, but we need exactly 2 sunny lines. Could we use all n lines that are not sunny? That would be 3 families, but we have only n lines; we could potentially use all horizontal lines (n lines) and no sunny lines, giving k=0. For k=1, we used n-1 horizontal lines and 1 sunny line. For k=2, maybe we can use n-2 horizontal lines and 2 sunny lines that are both "steep" lines that cover the remaining points? But as we saw, the remaining points are not collinear on a sunny line. However, we could also include a vertical line as one of the non-sunny lines, and then the uncovered points might be arranged such that two sunny lines can cover them. Let's think systematically.

We need to cover all points in the triangle T = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2: a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1}. We have n lines L1,...,Ln. Exactly k are sunny. We want to know for which k such a set exists.

Observation: The non-sunny lines are parallel to x-axis (horizontal), y-axis (vertical), or line x+y=0 (slope -1). So each non-sunny line belongs to one of these three directions. Let's denote the set of non-sunny lines as H (horizontal), V (vertical), D (diagonal). They are distinct lines.

Consider the projection onto the x-axis or y-axis? The covering condition: each point (a,b) must be on some line. If we use a horizontal line y = c, it covers all points with that y-coordinate. Similarly vertical x = a covers all points with that x-coordinate. Diagonal x+y = c covers all points with that sum.

We have n lines total. Suppose we have h horizontal lines, v vertical lines, d diagonal lines. Then h+v+d = n - k (since exactly k sunny lines). The lines are distinct. The coverage: The union of horizontal lines covers all points with y-coordinate in H (set of y-values). Similarly vertical lines cover points with x-coordinate in V. Diagonal lines cover points with sum in D (set of sums). The sunny lines may cover other points.

We need to cover all points in T. Let's denote the sets: For each y from 1 to n, the points with that y are those with a from 1 to n+1-y. So if we have horizontal lines y = c for c in some set H, then all points with y in H are covered. Similarly for x. For sum s from 2 to n+1, points with a+b=s are covered if s is in D (the set of sums for diagonal lines). So the uncovered points are those with y not in H, x not in V, and sum not in D. But also the sunny lines can cover some of these.

We need to see for which k we can choose H,V,D (with sizes h,v,d such that h+v+d = n-k) and a set of k sunny lines such that the union covers T.

Maybe there is a known result: For n lines covering the triangular grid points, the number of lines that are not parallel to the axes or x+y=0 must be at least something? Alternatively, we can consider the dual problem: each line corresponds to a point in the dual space? Not sure.

Let's try to find necessary conditions on k. Possibly k must be 0,1, or maybe n? Let's test small n.

We can try n=3. Then triangle T: points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤4. So points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). Total 6 points. We need 3 lines covering all these points, with exactly k sunny lines.

We can attempt to construct for each k=0,1,2,3.

k=0: take three horizontal lines y=1,2,3. Those cover all points: y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1); y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2); y=3 covers (1,3). All covered. So k=0 works.

k=1: take two horizontal lines y=1,2 and one sunny line through (1,3) maybe? But need to cover (3,1) and (2,2) and (1,3). Horizontal lines y=1,2 cover (1,1),(2,1),(3,1) and (1,2),(2,2). So uncovered points: (1,3). So a sunny line through (1,3) works. So k=1 works.

k=2: need exactly 2 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. Could we have 1 horizontal line and 2 sunny lines? Or 1 vertical and 2 sunny, etc. Try 1 horizontal line y=1. Then it covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1). Uncovered: (1,2),(1,3),(2,2). Need 2 sunny lines to cover these three points. Can two sunny lines cover these three points? The three points: (1,2),(1,3),(2,2). They are not collinear on a sunny line because any two determine a line: (1,2)-(1,3) vertical x=1 not sunny; (1,2)-(2,2) horizontal y=2 not sunny; (1,3)-(2,2) slope = (2-3)/(2-1) = -1, not sunny. So no two sunny lines can cover all three because any line through two points is non-sunny. Could we cover them with two sunny lines such that each line covers at most one point? Then one point uncovered. So not possible. Try 1 vertical line x=1. Covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3). Uncovered: (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). Again similar: any two determine non-sunny lines. So not possible. Try 1 diagonal line x+y=2? That covers (1,1) only (since sum=2). Or x+y=3 covers (1,2),(2,1). Or x+y=4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). That's 3 points. So if we take diagonal line x+y=4, it covers three points. Then uncovered: (1,1),(2,1)? Wait, x+y=4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). So uncovered points: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1). That's three points. Again any two determine non-sunny lines. So not possible. Thus k=2 seems impossible for n=3. What about using 1 horizontal and 2 sunny, but maybe the sunny lines are not required to cover the uncovered points solely; they could also cover some points that are already covered by the non-sunny line, but we need to cover all uncovered points. The uncovered points after taking 1 non-sunny line are the points not on that line. That's 6 - (# points on that line). For a horizontal line y=c, it covers (n+1-c) points? Actually for n=3, y=1 covers 3 points; y=2 covers 2; y=3 covers 1. So uncovered points count = 6 - (#points on line). For y=1: uncovered 3 points; for y=2: uncovered 4 points; for y=3: uncovered 5 points. Similarly vertical x=1: 3 points; x=2: 2 points; x=3: 1 point. Diagonal x+y=2: 1 point; x+y=3: 2 points; x+y=4: 3 points. So for k=2, we have 1 non-sunny line, and 2 sunny lines. We need to cover all uncovered points with the two sunny lines. Since we have only 2 sunny lines, they can cover at most 2*(some number) but also the uncovered points might be many. Could we choose the non-sunny line to be one that covers many points, leaving few uncovered? For n=3, the maximum points on a non-sunny line is 3 (horizontal y=1, vertical x=1, diagonal x+y=4). So uncovered = 3 points. So we need 2 sunny lines to cover 3 points. As argued, any line through two of those three points is non-sunny, so two sunny lines cannot cover all three because that would require at least one line covering two points, which would be non-sunny. Could we use a sunny line that covers one point and another sunny line that covers the other two? The line covering two points would be non-sunny, so not allowed. Could we use a sunny line that covers all three? That would require them to be collinear on a sunny line, but they are not collinear on a sunny line. So impossible. So k=2 does not work for n=3.

k=3: all three lines sunny. Could we cover all 6 points with three sunny lines? Possibly. For instance, take lines y = 2x? That passes through (1,2),(2,4) but (2,4) not in T. Not covering all. We need to cover each point. Since we have only 3 lines, each line can cover at most 3 points (since points are in a triangle). But we need to cover 6 points. So maybe each line covers exactly 2 points? That would be 6 points total. So we need three sunny lines, each covering exactly two points, and the union of these two-point sets covers all points. Is that possible? Let's list points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). We need three lines (s Sunny) such that each line contains at least two points, and the union is all points. Also the lines are distinct. Let's try to find such lines. A line with slope not 0,∞,-1. For instance, line through (1,1) and (2,2) is y=x (slope 1), which is sunny (slope 1 ≠ -1,0,∞). That line contains (1,1),(2,2). Also (3,3) not in T. So covers two points. Next, line through (1,2) and (2,1) is y = -x+3 (slope -1), not sunny. So not allowed. Line through (1,3) and (2,2) is y = -x+4? (3,2?) Actually (1,3) and (2,2): slope = (2-3)/(2-1) = -1, not sunny. So not allowed. Line through (3,1) and (2,2) is slope = (2-1)/(2-3)=1/(-1) = -1, not sunny. So we need to find sunny lines that cover points. Possibly we can take lines that are not through two points that are "axis-aligned" or diagonal. For example, line through (1,1) and (3,1) is horizontal y=1, not sunny. Through (1,1) and (1,2) vertical. Through (1,1) and (2,1) horizontal. So any line through two points that are either same x or same y or same sum is non-sunny. So the only pairs that give sunny lines are those where the slope is not 0, ∞, -1. So we need to find three sunny lines such that each line covers at least two points, and the union is all points. Let's list all possible sunny lines through two points. Compute slopes:

Points: A(1,1), B(1,2), C(1,3), D(2,1), E(2,2), F(3,1).

Pairs:
A-B: vertical x=1 (non-sunny)
A-C: vertical x=1 (non-sunny)
A-D: slope = (1-1)/(2-1)=0 => horizontal y=1 (non-sunny)
A-E: slope = (2-1)/(2-1)=1 => sunny (slope 1)
A-F: slope = (1-1)/(3-1)=0 => horizontal y=1 (non-sunny)
B-C: vertical x=1 (non-sunny)
B-D: slope = (1-2)/(2-1) = -1 => diagonal (non-sunny)
B-E: slope = (2-2)/(2-1)=0 => horizontal y=2 (non-sunny)
B-F: slope = (1-2)/(3-1) = -1/2 => sunny? slope -1/2, not 0,∞,-1, so sunny.
C-D: slope = (1-3)/(2-1) = -2 => sunny (slope -2)
C-E: slope = (2-3)/(2-1) = -1 => non-sunny (diagonal)
C-F: slope = (1-3)/(3-1) = -2/2 = -1 => non-sunny (diagonal)
D-E: slope = (2-1)/(2-2)=1/0 => vertical x=2 (non-sunny)
D-F: slope = (1-1)/(3-2)=0 => horizontal y=1 (non-sunny)
E-F: slope = (1-2)/(3-2) = -1 => diagonal (non-sunny)

So the sunny lines through two points are: A-E (slope 1), B-F (slope -1/2), C-D (slope -2). Also possibly A-E? Already listed. Also C-D is slope -2. That's three sunny lines. Do they cover all points? A-E covers A(1,1) and E(2,2). B-F covers B(1,2) and F(3,1). C-D covers C(1,3) and D(2,1). So the union covers all points: A,E,B,F,C,D. Yes! So with three sunny lines, we can cover all points. So k=3 works for n=3.

Thus for n=3, possible k: 0,1,3. k=2 not possible.

Now test n=4. Triangle T: a,b≥1, a+b≤5. Points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). Total 10 points. We need 4 lines covering all these points, with exactly k sunny lines.

We can try to construct for k=0,1,2,3,4.

k=0: use all 4 horizontal lines y=1,2,3,4? But note: For n=4, b max is 4 because a+b≤5 and a≥1 => b≤4. So horizontal lines y=1..4 cover all points: y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1); y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2); y=3 covers (1,3),(2,3); y=4 covers (1,4). So all covered. So k=0 works.

k=1: use 3 horizontal lines y=1,2,3 and 1 sunny line through (1,4) maybe? But need to cover all points. Horizontal lines y=1,2,3 cover points with b=1,2,3. Points with b=4: only (1,4). So sunny line through (1,4) works. So k=1 works.

k=2: need exactly 2 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines. Could we do similar: use 2 horizontal lines y=1,2 covering b=1,2. Then uncovered points: those with b=3,4. b=3: points (1,3),(2,3); b=4: (1,4). So three points: (1,3),(2,3),(1,4). Can two sunny lines cover these? As before, any two of these determine a line that is non-sunny? Check: (1,3)-(2,3) horizontal y=3, non-sunny; (1,3)-(1,4) vertical x=1, non-sunny; (2,3)-(1,4) slope = (4-3)/(1-2)=1/(-1)=-1, non-sunny. So any line through two of them is non-sunny. So two sunny lines cannot cover all three because that would require one line covering two points, which would be non-sunny. But could we cover them with two sunny lines such that one line covers one point and the other covers the other two? The line covering two points would be non-sunny, not allowed. So no.

Maybe we can choose a different set of non-sunny lines, not all horizontal. For instance, use 2 diagonal lines covering sums 2 and 3? That would cover points with sum=2: (1,1); sum=3: (1,2),(2,1). That's 3 points. Uncovered: points with sum=4,5. Sum=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1); sum=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). That's 7 points. Two sunny lines cannot cover all 7.

What about using 2 vertical lines? x=1,2? Then they cover points (1,1)-(1,4) and (2,1)-(2,3) (since x=2, y≤3 because 2+y≤5 => y≤3). So uncovered: points with x=3,4 and y such that sum≤5: (3,1),(3,2),(4,1). That's 3 points. Again any two of these? (3,1)-(3,2) vertical x=3, non-sunny; (3,1)-(4,1) horizontal y=1, non-sunny; (3,2)-(4,1) slope = (1-2)/(4-3) = -1, non-sunny. So same issue.

What about using 1 horizontal and 1 vertical? Then we have 2 non-sunny lines, and 2 sunny lines. Could we cover all points? Let's see: Choose horizontal y=1, vertical x=1. Then they cover (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1) and (1,2),(1,3),(1,4). So covered points: all with y=1 or x=1. Uncovered points: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) is covered? (4,1) is y=1 so covered. Actually (3,2) not covered because x=3,y=2: x≠1, y≠1; sum=5? Actually a+b=3+2=5, so point (3,2) is in T. Uncovered: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). Also (3,1) covered by y=1, (1,4) covered by x=1, (4,1) covered by y=1, (2,1) covered by y=1, (1,2) covered by x=1, etc. So uncovered: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). That's 3 points. Again any two of these: (2,2)-(2,3) vertical x=2, non-sunny; (2,2)-(3,2) horizontal y=2, non-sunny; (2,3)-(3,2) slope = (2-3)/(3-2) = -1, non-sunny. So no two sunny lines can cover all three. We need to cover all points with n lines. For k=2, we have 2 sunny lines. Could we have a construction that uses only 2 sunny lines and some non-sunny lines that cover all points? Perhaps we can use a different selection of non-sunny lines that leaves a set of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines. Let's think more systematically.

We want to cover all points in T. The total number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. We have n lines. Each line can be thought of as a set of points it contains from T. The covering condition is that the union of these sets is T.

We have exactly k sunny lines. The rest are non-sunny lines (from H,V,D). The non-sunny lines are limited in the directions they can cover. Perhaps we can use a combinatorial argument to bound k.

Observe that each non-sunny line (horizontal, vertical, or diagonal) is "axis-aligned" in the sense that its points have either constant y, constant x, or constant sum. These families are natural.

Let's denote the set of points in T. Consider the projection onto the x-axis: For each x from 1 to n, the points with that x are those with b from 1 to n+1-x. So the vertical lines x = a cover these columns. Similarly, horizontal lines y = b cover rows.

Now, if we have a line that is not sunny, it belongs to one of these three families. The sunny lines can be arbitrary lines with slope not 0, ∞, -1.

We need to find for which k there exists a covering with exactly k sunny lines.

From small n, k=0 works, k=1 works, k=3 works (for n=3,4,5). k=2 fails for n=3,4,5. Let's test n=6 quickly to see if k=2 becomes possible. n=6, triangle T: a,b≥1, a+b≤7. Points: (1,1)-(6,1):6 points; b=2: (1,2)-(5,2):5; b=3: (1,3)-(4,3):4; b=4: (1,4)-(3,4):3; b=5: (1,5)-(2,5):2; b=6: (1,6):1. Total N = 21. Need 6 lines.

We want to see if we can have exactly 2 sunny lines and 4 non-sunny lines covering all points. Could we use 4 horizontal lines y=1,2,3,4? That covers b=1:6 points; b=2:5; b=3:4; b=4:3; total 18 points. Uncovered: b=5:2 points; b=6:1 point; total 3 points. Again three points uncovered. Need 2 sunny lines to cover 3 points. As before, any two of these three points determine a non-sunny line. So two sunny lines cannot cover all three. Could we use a different set of 4 non-sunny lines? For instance, use 2 horizontal and 2 vertical? Let's try to cover as many points as possible with non-sunny lines, leaving a set that can be covered by 2 sunny lines. The maximum points per non-sunny line is 6 (horizontal y=1, vertical x=1, diagonal sum=7? sum=7: a from 1 to 6, b=7-a, so (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1) -> 6 points). So each non-sunny line can cover up to 6 points. With 4 non-sunny lines, we could potentially cover up to 24 points if disjoint, but we only have 21 points, so it's possible to cover all points if we can arrange them to be disjoint (no overlapping points). Is it possible to have 4 non-sunny lines (from H,V,D) that are pairwise disjoint in T and together cover all 21 points? That would require the lines to be chosen so that no point lies on two of them, and the union is T. Since we have 4 lines, and each covers at most 6 points, total distinct points covered ≤ 24, but we need exactly 21. So it's plausible. However, can we actually achieve that? Let's try to construct.

We have three families: H, V, D. Each line in a family is defined by a parameter (y for H, x for V, sum s for D). The sets of points they contain are: H_y: {(a,y): a=1..n+1-y}, V_x: {(x,b): b=1..n+1-x}, D_s: {(a,b): a+b=s, a,b≥1}. The triangle T has points with a+b ≤ n+1. The union of H_y and V_x will have all points with a=some x or b=some y. To be disjoint, we need that no point satisfies both conditions. That is, for any point (a,b) in T, it cannot be on both H_y and V_x if y=b and x=a? Actually if a point is on H_y, then b=y; on V_x, then a=x. So the intersection of H_y and V_x is (x,y) if x+y ≤ n+1. So to avoid intersection, we need that for any chosen horizontal line y=c and vertical line x=d, we have d+c > n+1. So if we choose both a horizontal and a vertical line, we must ensure their intercepts sum > n+1. That is possible, e.g., for n=6, take y=6 (covers (1,6) only), x=6 (covers (6,1) only), then 6+6=12 >7, so intersection (6,6) not in T because sum=12>7. So they are disjoint.

Similarly, for horizontal and diagonal: intersection of y=c and x+y=s gives (s-c,c). This point is in T if s-c ≥1 and c≥1 and s-c+c=s ≤ n+1. So to avoid intersection, we need either s-c <1 or s > n+1 (but s ≤ n+1 for diagonal covering points in T? Actually we can choose diagonal lines with s between 2 and n+1 to cover points; s > n+1 would have no points in T). So we need s-c <1 => c > s-1. Since c ≤ n, and s ≤ n+1, it's possible to choose c > s-1. For instance, for n=6, take y=6 and diagonal s=7? s=7 gives points (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1). Intersection with y=6: (1,6) which is in T (since 1+6=7≤7). So that's an overlap. To avoid, we could choose y=5 and s=7: intersection (2,5) sum=7, point in T? 2+5=7≤7, yes, so overlap. y=4 and s=7: (3,4) sum=7, point in T. y=3 and s=7: (4,3) sum=7, point in T. y=2 and s=7: (5,2) sum=7, point in T. y=1 and s=7: (6,1) sum=7, point in T. So any horizontal line with y between 1 and 6 will intersect the diagonal s=7 at a point in T? Actually for y=1, intersection (6,1) is in T because 6+1=7≤7. So all horizontal lines y=c with c≥1 intersect the diagonal s=7 at (7-c,c) provided 7-c ≥1 and c≥1. For c=1..6, 7-c≥1, so yes. So to avoid intersection, we would need to choose a diagonal line with s > n+1? Then no points in T, so not useful. Or choose a horizontal line with y > s-1? For s=7, y >6 means y≥7, but y max is 6, so impossible. So if we include the diagonal s=7 (the longest diagonal), it will intersect every horizontal line y=c with c from 1 to 6 at a point in T. So to have disjointness, we cannot have both a horizontal line and the diagonal s=7. Similarly, for vertical lines and diagonal: intersection of x=a and x+y=s gives (a, s-a). This point is in T if a≥1, s-a≥1, and s ≤ n+1. So for s=n+1, vertical lines x=a with a=1..n will intersect at (a, n+1-a) which is in T because a + (n+1-a)=n+1≤n+1. So again, if we include the diagonal s=n+1, it intersects every vertical line. So to have disjointness, we cannot include both a vertical line and the diagonal s=n+1. Similarly, for two diagonals: two different sums s1 and s2, they are disjoint because they have no points in common (since points have unique sum). So two diagonals are automatically disjoint.

Thus, to have a set of non-sunny lines that are pairwise disjoint in T, we must avoid combinations that cause overlap. The overlaps occur when:
- Horizontal y=c and vertical x=d: intersection (d,c) is in T iff d+c ≤ n+1.
- Horizontal y=c and diagonal x+y=s: intersection (s-c,c) is in T iff s-c ≥1 and c≥1 and s ≤ n+1 (but if s ≤ n+1, s-c could be <1? Actually if s-c <1, then intersection is not a point of T because x positive; so we need s-c ≥1). So overlap if s-c ≥1 and c≥1 and s ≤ n+1.
- Vertical x=a and diagonal x+y=s: intersection (a, s-a) is in T iff a≥1, s-a≥1, and s ≤ n+1.

So to avoid overlaps, we need to choose lines such that for any pair, the intersection point is not in T (i.e., either coordinates not positive or sum > n+1). Since we only consider lines that actually contain points in T (otherwise they are useless), we want lines that are "far apart".

Thus k=4 fails for n=5.

For n=4, k=4 we already saw fails because we only have 4 lines, each sunny line can cover at most 2 points (since y=x covers (1,1),(2,2); y=2x covers (1,2); etc.), total max points covered ≤ 8, but we need 10 points, so impossible.

Thus k=4 seems impossible for n=4,5,6. What about k=5 for n=5? That would be 5 sunny lines and 0 non-sunny lines. With 5 lines, can we cover all 15 points? Each sunny line can cover at most 3 points (y=x covers 3 points; y=2x covers 2; etc.). So 5 lines could cover at most 15 points if each covers exactly 3 points and they are disjoint. For n=5, we can try to use lines like y=x (covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3)); y=x+1 (covers (1,2),(2,3) but (3,4) not in T; so only 2 points); y=x+2 (covers (1,3),(2,4) but (3,5) not; 2 points); y=x+3 (covers (1,4),(2,5) but (3,6) not; 2 points); y=2x (covers (1,2) and (2,4) maybe? So with 5 lines, the maximum total points covered is 3 + 2*4 = 11, less than 15. So impossible. Thus k=5 fails.

So far, for n=3, possible k:0,1,3; for n=4:0,1,3; for n=5:0,1,3; for n=6:0,1,3? We need to test n=6 k=3. We already have a construction for n=6 k=3? Possibly similar to n=5 k=3 using y=1,2,3 as non-sunny lines and then sunny lines covering the remaining points? For n=6, take non-sunny lines y=1,2,3 (covers b=1:6, b=2:5, b=3:4 -> total 15 points).
