Thus it appears that for n≥3, k=0,1,3 are always achievable. We need to check if k=2 is ever possible. We have seen it fails for n=3,4,5,6. Could it work for larger n? Let's try to reason generally.

We need to cover all points in T. Suppose we have exactly k sunny lines. For k=2, we have 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are from H,V,D. The covering condition is that every point in T is on at least one of these lines.

Observation: The non-sunny lines are axis-aligned or slope -1. They are "directions" that are natural. Perhaps we can use a combinatorial argument about the number of points on each "level". For a given y, the points (a,y) are covered by a horizontal line y = constant. So if we have a horizontal line at y = c, it covers all points with that y. Similarly, vertical lines cover all points with a given x. Diagonal lines cover all points with a given sum.

Now, consider the set of points in T. They form a triangular array. We want to cover them with n lines. Since we have n lines total, and there are N = n(n+1)/2 points, on average each line covers about (n+1)/2 points. The sunny lines might cover fewer points because they are not "parallel" to the axes or x+y=0; they can only contain at most floor((n+1)/2) points? Actually let's compute the maximum number of integer points on a line with slope not 0,∞,-1 within T. For a line y = mx + c, with m rational, the integer points are of the form (x, mx+c). Since the triangle has bounded coordinates, the maximum number of points is limited. For m=1, the line y = x + b gives points (a, a+b) with a ≥ 1, a+b ≥ 1, a + (a+b) ≤ n+1 => 2a+b ≤ n+1. So a can go up to floor((n+1-b)/2). For b=0, a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So max points = floor((n+1)/2). For m=2, line y = 2x + c gives points (a, 2a+c) with sum a+2a+c=3a+c ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1-c)/3). For c=0, a ≤ floor((n+1)/3). So fewer. For m=-2, line y = -2x + c gives sum a + (-2a+c)=c-a ≤ n+1 => a ≥ c - (n+1). Also a≥1, so limited. For m=-1/2, similar. So the maximum points on a sunny line is at most floor((n+1)/2) (achieved by y=x). Actually y=x covers points (a,a) with a ≤ floor((n+1)/2) because sum=2a ≤ n+1. So at most floor((n+1)/2). For n=3, floor(4/2)=2; we found sunny lines covering 2 points. For n=4, floor(5/2)=2; we had sunny lines covering 2 points. For n=5, floor(6/2)=3; we had y=x covering 3 points. For n=6, floor(7/2)=3; we had y=x covering 3 points? Actually y=x: points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) for n=6 (since sum=6≤7), so 3 points. So max points per sunny line is about n/2. But note that in our constructions for k=3, we used sunny lines that covered at most 2 points each for n=4,5,6? For n=4, we used sunny lines covering 2 points; for n=5, we used sunny lines covering 2 points; for n=6, we used sunny lines covering 2 points? Actually we used y=x+3 covering 2 points; y=-2x+8 covering 2 points; y=-1/2 x+9/2 covering 2 points. So they covered 2 points each. So the maximum points per sunny line is not always attained; it depends on the arrangement.

Now, for k=2, we have 2 sunny lines. Their total coverage can be at most 2*floor((n+1)/2) ≈ n, but we have n-2 non-sunny lines that can cover many points. However, the non-sunny lines are limited in the sense that they can only cover points along rows, columns, or diagonals. Perhaps we can show that if there are exactly 2 sunny lines, then the total number of points covered by the non-sunny lines is at most something, leaving at least 3 points uncovered that cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines.

Alternatively, we can consider the following: For any line L (not sunny), its points in T have either constant y, constant x, or constant sum. The union of all such lines must cover T. Suppose we have n lines total, with exactly k sunny lines. The complementary set of points (those not on non-sunny lines) must be covered by sunny lines. If we have many non-sunny lines, they may cover most points. But we need to find a necessary condition on k.

Let's denote by R the set of rows (y-values) that have a horizontal line present. Similarly, C for columns (x-values), and D for diagonals (sums). Then the points covered by non-sunny lines are those with y in R, or x in C, or sum in D. The sunny lines cover the remaining points.

We need to cover all points. Consider the points with maximum x or y. For instance, point (n,1) (the bottom right corner) has sum = n+1. This point is covered by a horizontal line y=1 if we have that; by a vertical line x=n if we have that; by a diagonal sum=n+1 if we have that. Similarly, point (1,n) is covered by y=n, x=1, sum=n+1. So to cover these corners, we likely need at least one of these families.

Now, for k=2, we have 2 sunny lines. Could we choose non-sunny lines to cover all points except a set of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines? Let's try to see if it's possible for large n.

Suppose we take the non-sunny lines to be all horizontal lines y=1,...,n-2? That would cover b=1..n-2, leaving b=n-1 and n. That's n-1 and n rows. The points in those rows: For b=n-1, a from 1 to 2 (since a+(n-1)≤n+1 => a≤2). So (1,n-1),(2,n-1). For b=n, a=1 only. So three points uncovered. Could two sunny lines cover these three points? As argued, any line through two of them is non-sunny, so each sunny line can contain at most one of these three points (since if a sunny line contains two, that line would be non-sunny). Thus two sunny lines can cover at most 2 points, leaving 1 uncovered. So that fails. But maybe we can choose a different set of non-sunny lines that cover more than those three points? For instance, we could include a diagonal line that covers one of these points, reducing the uncovered set size. But we have only 3 non-sunny lines (since n-2=3 for k=2? Actually for n=5, we have 3 non-sunny lines; for n=6, we have 4 non-sunny lines? Wait, k=2 means exactly 2 sunny lines, so total lines n, thus non-sunny lines = n-2. For n=6, n-2=4 non-sunny lines. So we have 4 non-sunny lines to cover points. Could we choose these 4 non-sunny lines to cover all points except maybe a set that can be covered by 2 sunny lines? The total points is 21; if we can cover 19 points with 4 non-sunny lines, then uncovered = 2 points, which can be covered by 1 sunny line (or 2 lines). But we have 2 sunny lines, so that's fine. So we need to see if there exists a selection of 4 non-sunny lines (from H,V,D) that covers 19 points, leaving only 2 points uncovered, and then we can cover those 2 points with 2 sunny lines (or one line). Is that possible? For n=6, we attempted with y=1,2,3, and diagonal s=7? That gave 18 covered, leaving 3 uncovered. What about y=1,2,4, and diagonal s=7? Let's try systematically.

We have to choose lines to maximize coverage. Since we have 4 lines, we can try different families. The total points are 21. The maximum points that can be covered by 4 lines is 21 if they are disjoint and cover all points. But can we achieve that? For a set of 4 lines from H,V,D to be disjoint, we need to avoid overlaps. Overlaps occur when a point lies on two lines. To cover all points, we need to cover each point at least once. Since we have 4 lines, each covering some points, we need the union to be T. If we can cover all points with 4 non-sunny lines, then k=0 works. So for k=2, we need to use 2 sunny lines, which means we are forced to include them; but we could still cover all points with the non-sunny lines, and then the sunny lines are extra? No, we need exactly k sunny lines, so we must include 2 sunny lines. But if we can cover all points with non-sunny lines, we could simply not include any sunny lines, giving k=0. For k=2, we need to include 2 sunny lines, but they could be redundant (they cover points already covered). However, the condition says exactly k lines are sunny; the lines themselves can be any lines. So we could have a configuration where the 2 sunny lines are not needed for coverage, but we still include them. That would give k=2, but we need to see if we can have a set of n lines that includes 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines that together cover T. It doesn't matter if some lines are redundant; the covering condition is just that every point is on at least one line. So we could take the construction for k=0 (e.g., all horizontal lines) and then add two sunny lines (distinct from them) that are not needed. But then we would have n+2 lines? Wait, we need exactly n lines total. For k=0, we have n lines (all non-sunny). To get k=2, we need to have exactly 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. If we take the n non-sunny lines from the k=0 construction, that's n lines, no sunny lines. To have k=2, we need to replace some of those non-sunny lines with sunny lines, or we need to have a different set of n lines where exactly 2 are sunny. Could we simply take the k=0 construction (n horizontal lines) and then replace two of those horizontal lines with two sunny lines that also cover the same points? But then the sunny lines would be distinct from the horizontals; but we need the total number of lines to be n, so if we replace two horizontals with two sunny lines, we still have n lines. But the covering condition: the set of lines now includes the sunny lines and the remaining horizontals. The union of all lines is still T because the horizontals cover all points. So that works: for any n, we can take n-2 horizontal lines (y=1..n-2) and 2 sunny lines that are arbitrary (smooth, not parallel to axes or x+y=0). But then we must ensure that the sunny lines are distinct from the horizontals; they can be any lines. However, we also need that the total number of lines is n. If we take n-2 horizontals, that's n-2 lines; then we need 2 more lines, which are sunny. So total n lines. But are the horizontals covering all points? No, as we saw, horizontals y=1..n-2 cover b=1..n-2, but points with b=n-1 and n are uncovered. So the union of the horizontals alone is not T; we need the sunny lines to cover those points. So we cannot just take any sunny lines; they must cover the uncovered points. So the construction for k=2 would require that the n-2 non-sunny lines (which could be horizontals) cover most points, and the 2 sunny lines cover the rest. That's what we attempted. But we could choose the non-sunny lines to be a mix that covers all points except a small set, and then the sunny lines cover that small set. For k=2, we need to cover all points with 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. Since n-2 non-sunny lines can cover at most n-2 rows if they are horizontals, but we could also use verticals and diagonals to cover points more efficiently.

Let's consider the possibility of covering all points with only 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. For large n, the total points N = n(n+1)/2 is about n^2/2. The n-2 non-sunny lines, each can cover at most n points (if horizontal y=1, vertical x=1, or diagonal sum=n+1). So the maximum total points that could be covered by these lines, if they are disjoint, is (n-2)*n ≈ n^2. That's larger than N, so in principle they could cover all points if they are chosen appropriately and if they are disjoint. But are they disjoint? For instance, if we take all horizontal lines y=1,...,n-2, they are disjoint, covering points with b=1..n-2, which is about n(n-1)/2 points. That's about n^2/2 - n/2. The uncovered points are those with b=n-1 and n, which is about n+1 points? Actually b=n-1: points with a=1,2 (2 points), b=n: 1 point, total 3 points for n=5, but for general n, b=n-1 gives a=1,2? Since a+(n-1) ≤ n+1 => a ≤ 2, so 2 points; b=n gives a=1 only; total 3 points. So only 3 points uncovered. That's a very small number. For large n, the uncovered set size is 3 (the points (1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n) if n≥3). That's only 3 points. So with n-2 horizontals, we cover all but 3 points. Then we need 2 sunny lines to cover these 3 points. As argued, any line through two of these points is non-sunny, so each sunny line can cover at most 1 of these 3 points (since if it covers 2, that line is non-sunny). Therefore, with 2 sunny lines, we can cover at most 2 points, leaving 1 point uncovered. So that fails. However, maybe we can choose the non-sunny lines to be not all horizontals; we could include a diagonal or vertical line that covers some of these 3 points, reducing the uncovered set to 2 points. If we take y=1..n-3 (that's n-3 lines) and x=1 (1 line), total n-2 lines. The union of these: horizontals cover points with b=1..n-3; vertical x=1 covers points (1,b) for b=1..n. So combined, they cover all points except those with b=n-2, n-1, n and a>1. Let's compute. For n=5, y=1,2 (n-3=2 lines), x=1 gives union: y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1); y=2: (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2); x=1: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5). Thus for n=5, k=2 fails. For n=6, we tried y=1,2,3 (3 lines) and then 2 sunny lines; but we had 3 non-sunny lines, not 4. For n=6, k=2 means 4 non-sunny lines. We have three families. The points in T form a triangular grid. The union of all lines from one family (e.g., all horizontals) covers all points except those with b=n-1 and b=n. That's 3 points. So with n lines (all horizontals), we cover N-3 points. With n-1 horizontals, we cover N-3 points? Actually if we take n-1 horizontals y=1..n-1, they cover all points except those with b=n, which are only (1,n) (since a+ n ≤ n+1 => a≤1). So that's 1 point uncovered. So with n-1 horizontals, we cover N-1 points. That's a good observation: taking all horizontals except the top one (y=n) leaves only (1,n) uncovered. That gives k=0? Actually if we take n-1 horizontals, we have n-1 lines, all non-sunny, covering N-1 points. Then we need 1 more line to cover the remaining point. That line could be sunny (or non-sunny, but if we add a horizontal y=n, that would be n lines, all non-sunny, covering all points). For k=1, we used n-1 horizontals and one sunny line through (1,n). That's valid. For k=2, we would need n-2 non-sunny lines. If we take n-2 horizontals y=1..n-2, they cover all points except those with b=n-1 and b=n. That's 2 rows: b=n-1 gives 2 points (1,n-1),(2,n-1); b=n gives (1,n). So 3 points uncovered. Could we cover those 3 points with 2 sunny lines? As argued, any line through two of them is non-sunny, so each sunny line can contain at most 1 of them. So 2 sunny lines can cover at most 2, leaving 1 uncovered. So that fails.

But maybe we can choose the n-2 non-sunny lines not all horizontals; we could include a vertical line that covers one of these points, thereby reducing the uncovered set to 2 points. For instance, take n-2 non-sunny lines: horizontals y=1..n-3 (n-3 lines) and one vertical line x=1 (1 line). That totals n-2 lines. Their union covers points with b=1..n-3 (all points in those rows) plus column a=1 (points (1,b) for b=1..n). Which points are uncovered? Points with b≥n-2 and a≥2. For b=n-2: a from 1 to 3? Since a+(n-2)≤n+1 => a≤3. So (1,n-2) covered by x=1; (2,n-2) and (3,n-2) are uncovered. For b=n-1: a from 1 to 2; (1,n-1) covered by x=1; (2,n-1) uncovered. For b=n: a=1 only; (1,n) covered by x=1. Now, we can try to prove the general statement: For any n≥3, the maximum number of points in T that can be covered by n-2 lines from H,V,D is N-3. Alternatively, we can consider the dual: Each non-sunny line is either horizontal, vertical, or diagonal. The points in T have coordinates. Maybe we can use a combinatorial argument based on the fact that the set of points in T can be partitioned into n "levels" by the sum? Not exactly.

Let's consider the projection onto the x-axis. Each vertical line x=a covers the points with that a. Each horizontal line y=b covers points with that b. Each diagonal line s covers points with that sum. The points in T are such that a+b ≤ n+1.

We could try to prove that the minimum number of non-sunny lines needed to cover T is n. That is, you cannot cover all points with fewer than n lines from H,V,D. Now, if the minimum number is n, then any set of n-2 lines cannot cover T. But could they cover all but 2 points? That would mean that the uncovered set size is 2. That would imply that there exists a set of n-2 lines that covers N-2 points. That does not contradict the minimum being n because the minimum is about covering all points, not covering most. So we need to check if such a set exists for some n. For n=5, we saw that max union from 3 lines is 12, which is N-3. So they leave 3 uncovered. Could there be a set that leaves only 2 uncovered? That would require union 13. We attempted many combos and got 12 max. So it seems the maximum union from n-2 lines is N-3. For n=6, max union from 4 lines is 18 = N-3. So likely the maximum union from n-2 lines is N-3. This is a stronger statement: The maximum number of points you can cover with n-2 non-sunny lines is N-3. That would mean you cannot cover more than N-3 points with n-2 lines. Then with 2 sunny lines, you can cover at most 2 of the remaining 3 points, leaving at least 1 uncovered. So k=2 is impossible.

But we need to prove that for any n≥3, the maximum union size from n-2 non-sunny lines is N-3. This is equivalent to saying that for any set of n-2 lines from H,V,D, there are at least 3 points of T not on any of them. Since we have a candidate set of 3 points: (1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n). We can try to show that these three points are always such that at least one is not covered. But more importantly, that the total number of uncovered points is at least 3. Perhaps we can use a combinatorial inequality: For each line, the number of points it covers is at most n (since the maximum size is n for H1, V1, D_{n+1}). The sum of sizes of n-2 lines is at most (n-2)n. However, we need to subtract overlaps. Overlaps occur when a point is on two lines. To get a union of size N, we would need the sum minus overlaps to be N. But N is about n^2/2. So if we have n-2 lines, the sum of sizes is about n^2, which is twice N. So to get a union of size N-2, the overlaps must be at least about n^2 - (N-2) ≈ n^2 - n^2/2 = n^2/2. That is a large number of overlaps. So it might be impossible to have such large overlaps because each line can only intersect others in a limited way. Let's try to bound the maximum possible union size from m lines.

We have three families. Lines from the same family are disjoint (since they have different parameters). So if we take a set of lines, we can consider how many from each family. Let h, v, d be the numbers of horizontals, verticals, diagonals chosen. Then h+v+d = m. The union size is at most the total number of points covered, which can be computed as:

- Horizontal lines: each horizontal line y=c covers points (a,c) for a=1..n+1-c. So the set of points covered by horizontals is the union of rows 1..n. If we take multiple horizontals, they are disjoint, so they cover exactly the sum of their row sizes. So contribution = sum_{c in H} (n+1-c). Similarly, verticals: sum_{a in V} (n+1-a). Diagonals: sum_{s in D} (s-1). But these contributions may overlap because a point can be on a horizontal and a vertical, or a horizontal and a diagonal, etc. The total union size = (sum of sizes) - (overlaps). Overlaps are points that lie on at least two lines.

To maximize union, we want to minimize overlaps. The overlaps occur when a point satisfies two conditions. For example, a point (a,b) is on a horizontal y=b and a vertical x=a, so it's an overlap between H and V. It is also on a horizontal y=b and a diagonal x+y=s if s=a+b. Similarly for vertical and diagonal. So to avoid overlaps, we would want to choose lines that are "far apart" so that their intersections lie outside T. But we have to cover many points; we cannot avoid overlaps entirely. The maximum union size from m lines is something like N - (something). We need to find the maximum possible union size for m=n-2.

Let's denote the set of points in T as a grid. Perhaps we can use the concept of "covering number" of a triangular grid by axis-aligned lines. It is known that the minimum number of axis-aligned lines needed to cover all points is n (since each row has at most n points, but the total number of rows is n; you can cover all points by taking all rows, which is n lines, but you cannot do with fewer because if you take n-1 rows, you miss at least one row's points, and those points are not all covered by the remaining lines? Actually if you take n-1 rows, you miss one row, say y=k. The points in that row are (1,k) to (n+1-k,k). Those points are not covered by the horizontals because we didn't include that row; they could be covered by verticals or diagonals. Could we cover all points in the missing row using only verticals and diagonals? Possibly, but we have only n-1 lines total, so we need to cover all points. The total points is N. If we take n-1 horizontals, they cover N - (n+1-k) points? Actually if we take all horizontals except one, we cover N - (n+1-k) points (since the missing row has n+1-k points). Then we need to cover those points with verticals and diagonals. But we have no more lines; we already used n-1 horizontals, so we have no verticals or diagonals. So we can't cover the missing row. So we need to use a mix. The minimum number of lines is n; we saw that with n lines, you can cover all points by taking all rows (n lines) or all columns (n lines) or all diagonals (n lines). So the minimum is n.

Thus we conjecture: For any n≥3, the maximum number of points in T that can be covered by n-2 lines from H,V,D is N-3. This is a plausible result.

We need to prove it. Let's attempt a general proof.

Let T_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1}. Let H = {y=c : c=1..n} (horizontals), V = {x=a : a=1..n} (verticals), D = {x+y=s : s=2..n+1} (diagonals). For a set L of lines, define U(L) = T_n ∩ (∪_{L∈L} L). We want to show that for any L with |L| = n-2, |U(L)| ≤ N - 3, where N = n(n+1)/2.

Now, we need to prove that for any n, the maximum number of points in T_n that can be covered by n-2 lines is N-3. This is equivalent to: For any set of n-2 lines from H,V,D, there exist at least 3 points of T_n not covered. Which 3? Possibly (1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n). But we need to show that these three are not all covered? Actually if we cover all three, we still have other points uncovered. But we need to show that at least 3 points are uncovered in total. So we need to show that the union cannot have size N-2 or N-1. So it's a bound.

One approach: Consider the "sum" of points covered by the lines. The total number of points in T_n is N. Each line can be thought of as a set of points. We can use a "charging" argument: For each point not covered, we assign it to some line that could have covered it but didn't. Or we can use the fact that the three points (1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n) are the only points that have b≥n-1. For any line L, if L covers any of these points, it must be from a specific family. Let's define the "top row" y=n-1 and y=n. The points in these rows are exactly the three points. Now, suppose we have a set L of m lines. The total number of points in T_n is N. The number of points covered by L is at most something like: The sum of sizes of the lines minus the number of intersections among them. The intersections occur when a point lies on two lines. To maximize coverage, we want to minimize intersections. However, we have to cover many points; the top region might force intersections.

Another angle: Use the concept of "covering number" of a triangular grid by lines. It is known that the minimum number of lines needed to cover all points of a triangular grid of side n is n. Moreover, the maximum number of points you can cover with n-1 lines is N-2? Actually for a grid of points (i,j) with i,j from 1 to n, the minimum number of lines to cover all points is n (by taking all rows). But with n-1 lines, you can cover n(n-1) points? Not exactly. For a square grid, the minimum number of axis-aligned lines to cover all points is n (rows). With n-1 rows, you cover n(n-1) points, leaving n points uncovered. So the maximum coverage with n-1 lines is n(n-1). That's N - n. For triangular grid, the pattern is similar but with the top corner.

We can attempt to prove by induction or by a combinatorial inequality.

Let L be a set of lines. For each line, define its "size" (number of points in T_n it contains). For horizontals: size = n+1-c. For verticals: size = n+1-a. For diagonals: size = s-1. Note that all these sizes are between 1 and n. The sum of sizes is at most (n-2)n. But we need to subtract overlaps. Overlaps occur when a point is on two lines. Let's count the number of overlapping points. Each pair of lines from different families can intersect at a point. Overlaps between same family are disjoint. So total overlaps = sum over pairs (L_i, L_j) of 1 if they intersect at a point in T_n. However, we need to consider that a point can be on more than two lines, so overlapping count is not simply the number of pairs; we need to use inclusion-exclusion. But for maximizing union, we want to minimize overlaps. So the maximum union is achieved when the lines are chosen to have as few intersections as possible. The intersections that are unavoidable might be those that involve the top points.

Perhaps we can show that any set of n-2 lines must have at least 3 intersections with each other (i.e., at least 3 points that are on two lines). But we need to show that the union size is at most N-3. Since N is the total points, if we have at least 3 points that are not covered, then union ≤ N-3. So we need to show that there are at least 3 points not covered by L.

Suppose L covers all but at most 2 points. Then the uncovered set has size 0,1,2. If it has size 0, then L covers T_n. But we know that the minimum number of lines to cover T_n is n, so with n-2 lines it's impossible to cover all points. So that case is ruled out. If it has size 1, then there is exactly one point not covered. Could that happen? For n=3, with 1 line, we can cover 3 points, leaving 3 uncovered. So not 1. For n=4, with 2 lines, we can cover 7 points, leaving 3 uncovered. So not 1. For n=5, with 3 lines, max coverage 12, leaving 3 uncovered. So not 1. For n=6, with 4 lines, max 18, leaving 3 uncovered. One could attempt to prove that the three points (1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n) are always uncovered. Is that true? Not necessarily; we can have a line that covers them, e.g., horizontal y=n-1 covers (1,n-1),(2,n-1); but then (1,n) is uncovered. So at least one of them is uncovered. Let's try to derive a general inequality. Consider the set of points in T_n. They can be partitioned into n "levels" by the sum of coordinates? Perhaps we can use a "covering" argument: Each line can cover at most n points. With m lines, the total number of points covered (counting multiplicities) is at most m*n. We need a more precise bound on the union size. Perhaps we can use the following idea: For each point (a,b) in T, consider the line that is "parallel" to one of the three directions. So we need to prove that for any selection of n-2 lines, |U(L)| ≤ N-3.

We can try to prove by considering the "complement" of U(L). Let's denote the set of points in T_n that are not covered by L. We want to show that this set has at least 3 points. Consider the three specific points: P1=(1,n-1), P2=(2,n-1), P3=(1,n). If all three are not covered, then we have at least 3 uncovered points. If some of them are covered, we need to show that there are still at least 3 uncovered points elsewhere. So we need to show that covering these top points forces the omission of at least 3 other points.

One can use a "pigeonhole" argument: For each line, the number of points it covers is at most n. The total number of points in T_n is N. If we have m lines, the maximum possible union size is at most N - (something). Actually we can use a bound: For any line L, define its "weight" as something. Or we can use a combinatorial inequality based on the fact that the three top points are "far apart" and each line can cover at most two of them. Now, we need to prove this in general. Let's attempt a proof by considering the "sum of b" of points covered. For each line L, define the total "b-sum" over its points. For a horizontal line y=c, the b-sum is c * (number of points) = c*(n+1-c). For a vertical line x=a, the b-sum is sum_{b=1}^{n+1-a} b = (n+1-a)(n+2-a)/2. For a diagonal line s=d, the b-sum is sum_{a=1}^{d-1} (d-a) = (d-1)d/2. So these are known.

Now, suppose we have a set L of m lines. The total b-sum over all points in T_n is S = sum_{b=1}^n b*(n+1-b) = sum_{b=1}^n (b(n+1) - b^2) = (n+1) sum b - sum b^2 = (n+1)*n(n+1)/2 - n(n+1)(2n+1)/6 = n(n+1)[(n+1)/2 - (2n+1)/6] = n(n+1)[ (3(n+1) - (2n+1))/6 ] = n(n+1)[(3n+3-2n-1)/6] = n(n+1)(n+2)/6. So S = n(n+1)(n+2)/6.

Now, the b-sum over the union U(L) is at most the sum of b-sums over the lines, but we need to subtract overlaps. However, if a point is covered by multiple lines, it will be counted multiple times in the sum of b-sums. To get the total b-sum over U(L) (i.e., sum of b over points that are in U(L)), we have: Sum_{points in U(L)} b = (Sum_{L∈L} b-sum(L)) - (overlap contributions). The overlap contributions are points that are on two lines, counted twice, so they are subtracted once when we take the union sum. But we don't know the overlaps. However, we can get a lower bound on the sum of b-sums of the lines if the union is large. Since each line's b-sum is positive, the sum of b-sums is at least something. If we want to cover many points, we need the total b-sum of the lines to be at least the sum of b over those points. But we have m lines; we can try to maximize the union size given a fixed total b-sum of lines? Not directly.

Thus it seems that for any n, the maximum number of points that can be covered by n-2 non-sunny lines is N-3. This is a plausible theorem. Then with k sunny lines, the total number of points covered is at most (N-3) + (some function of k). But we need to consider that the sunny lines can cover some of the uncovered points. Actually the union of all lines (non-sunny and sunny) must be T. The non-sunny lines cover at most N-3 points (since the best you can do with n-2 lines is N-3). Then the sunny lines must cover the remaining points. But the sunny lines can also cover points that are already covered by non-sunny lines, which doesn't help. So to cover all N points, the sunny lines must cover at least 3 points that are not covered by the non-sunny lines. So the union of sunny lines must contain at least 3 points that are not on any non-sunny line. But we have k sunny lines; each sunny line can contain at most something, but more importantly, we need to see if it's possible to cover those 3 points with k sunny lines. If k=1, we have 1 sunny line; can it cover at least 3 points that are not on any non-sunny line? Possibly, but we need to ensure that the non-sunny lines are chosen to leave a set of points that can be covered by 1 sunny line. For k=1, we can choose the non-sunny lines to cover all points except 1 point, and then a sunny line through that point covers it. But we need to cover all points; the non-sunny lines must cover N-1 points. Is it possible to cover N-1 points with n-1 non-sunny lines? Yes, by taking all horizontals except y=n: that covers rows 1..n-1, which is N-1 points. Then we need 1 sunny line to cover (1,n). That works. So k=1 is achievable. For k=2, we would need to cover at least 3 points with 2 sunny lines. But as we argued, the uncovered set from n-2 non-sunny lines is at least 3 points, and those 3 points cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines because any line through two of them is non-sunny. So each sunny line can cover at most 1 of them. Thus 2 sunny lines can cover at most 2 points, leaving at least 1 uncovered. So k=2 is impossible. For k=3, we need to cover at least 3 points with 3 sunny lines; that's possible if we can cover those 3 points with 3 sunny lines, but we need to ensure that the non-sunny lines leave exactly those 3 points. Another approach: For each point (a,b) in T, consider its "distance" from the origin. The lines we consider are of the form x = a, y = b, x+y = s. These are all lines that are either vertical, horizontal, or diagonal. The union of such lines can be seen as the set of points that satisfy at least one of these equations. The complement consists of points that avoid all these equations. In a triangle, there is a known result that the maximum number of points that can be covered by k such lines is N - (n - k) for k ≤ n? Actually for a square grid of side n, the minimum number of lines (rows and columns) to cover all points is n, and with n-1 lines you can cover n(n-1) points, leaving n points uncovered. So the uncovered count is n. For triangular grid, it's similar but with the diagonal family.

Let's analyze more systematically. Let T_n be the triangular grid of points. Let H, V, D be the three families. We want to choose n lines: h horizontals, v verticals, d diagonals (with h+v+d = n - k) and k sunny lines (which are not from H,V,D). The covering condition: Every point (a,b) in T_n is on at least one of the chosen lines.

We can think of the non-sunny lines as a set of lines from H,V,D that cover some points. The sunny lines are arbitrary lines with slopes not 0,∞,-1. They can be any lines. So the problem reduces to: For a given n, what are the possible numbers of sunny lines k for which we can cover T_n with n lines, of which exactly k are sunny? This is a combinatorial covering problem.

One approach: Consider the "extremal" points of T_n. The point (1,1) is the bottom-left corner. It is covered by H_1 (y=1), V_1 (x=1), D_2 (x+y=2). Similarly, the point (1,n) (top-left) is covered by H_n (y=n), V_1 (x=1), D_{n+1} (x+y=n+1). The point (n,1) (bottom-right) is covered by H_1 (y=1), V_n (x=n), D_{n+1} (x+y=n+1). So these corner points are natural.

We can use a "potential" function: For each line, define its "cost" as something. Perhaps we can derive a necessary condition on k by double counting the number of points covered by the lines. Another idea: Consider the sum over all points (a,b) of something like 1/(a+b). Not.

Given the time, I think we should look up or recall known results. This problem is likely from an IMO Shortlist. I recall a problem: "A line in the plane is called sunny if it is not parallel to the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0. Let n≥3 be an integer. Determine all nonnegative integers k for which there exist n distinct lines in the plane such that for all positive integers a,b with a+b≤n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines, and exactly k of the lines are sunny." b=4: (1,4)-(4,4) ->4 points; D8 has (4,4) yes; (3,4) not; (2,4) not; (1,4) not; so uncovered: (1,4),(2,4),(3,4) =3 points.
b=5: (1,5)-(3,5) ->3 points; D8 has (3,5) yes; (2,5) not; (1,5) not; so uncovered: (1,5),(2,5) =2 points.
b=6: (1,6)-(2,6) ->2 points; D8 has (2,6) yes; (1,6) not; so uncovered: (1,6) =1 point.
b=7: (1,7) ->1 point; D8 has (1,7) yes; so covered.
Thus uncovered points: (1,3),(2,3),(3,3),(4,3),(1,4),(2,4),(3,4),(1,5),(2,5),(1,6) -> total 10 points. So uncovered set is a triangle of size 5? Actually it's exactly T_5? T_5 has 15 points, not 10. This is a triangle of side 4? Points with b≥3: b=3:4, b=4:3, b=5:2, b=6:1; that's 10 points = T_4? T_4 has 10 points (since 4*5/2=10). Yes, that's T_4. So uncovered is T_4 (10 points). Then we have 4 sunny lines to cover T_4. As we argued, with 4 sunny lines you can cover at most 8 points of T_4 (since each sunny line can cover at most 2 points in T_4). So cannot cover all 10. So that construction fails.

Thus we need to find a set of 3 non-sunny lines that covers 25 points for n=7. The only way to cover 25 points is to have a union of size 25. The total points is 28, so uncovered 3. Is there a set of 3 lines from H,V,D that covers 25 points? The maximum coverage from 3 lines is what? Consider using H1,H2,H3: coverage =7+6+5=18, not 25. H1,H2,D8: 7+6+7-2=18. H1,D7,D8: D7=6, D8=7, H1=7; overlaps: H1& D8: (7,1); H1& D7: (6,1); D7& D8: none; so union =7+6+7-2=18. H1,H2,H4: 7+6+4=17. H1,H3,D8: 7+5+7-? overlaps: H1&D8: (7,1); H3&D8: (5,3); so union=7+5+7-2=17. So max seems 18. So cannot get 25. So k=4 fails for n=7? But we found a construction earlier for n=7 k=3, not k=4. Wait, we need to test n=7 k=4 again. We need 4 sunny lines, so m=3 non-sunny lines. With m=3, the best coverage is 18, leaving 10 points uncovered. Then we need 4 sunny lines to cover those 10 points. As argued, 4 sunny lines can cover at most 8 points of T_4. So impossible. Thus k=4 is impossible for n=7. But earlier we had a construction with H1,H2,D8 and then sunny lines covering the 10 uncovered points? That would be 4 sunny lines covering 10 points, which is impossible because max is 8. So that fails.

Thus for n=7, k=4 is not achievable. So the only possible k are 0,1,3. For n=3,4,5,6, we already have 0,1,3. So it seems that for all n≥3, the only possible k are 0,1,3. But we need to check n=7 again: Could there be a different set of 3 non-sunny lines that covers 25 points? The only way to get 25 is to have a union of size 25. The maximum union from 3 lines is 18 as we computed. Could we use a vertical line? For example, V1 (x=1) has 7 points, V2 (x=2) has 6, V3 (x=3) has 5; union=7+6+5=18. Mix: V1 and D8 and D7? V1=7, D8=7, D7=6; overlaps: V1&D8: (1,7); V1&D7: (1,6); D7&D8: none; union=7+7+6-2=18. So no. So 18 is max. Thus uncovered set size =28-18=10. So to cover those 10 with 4 sunny lines, we need each sunny line to cover at least 2.5 on average. But the maximum per sunny line in that region (T_4) is 2, so total max =8. So impossible. Thus k=4 fails for n=7.

What about k=5? m=2, best coverage 11 (H1+H2) or 13 (D8+D7) etc. So uncovered at least 17 points. Then 5 sunny lines max 10? No. So fails.

Thus the only k that work are 0,1,3 for all n≥3. But we should also check n=3: k=0 works (all horizontals), k=1 works (y=1,2 and sunny through (1,3)), k=3 works (we have construction). So that's fine.

Thus the answer: k can be 0, 1, or 3. For n=7, maybe k=4 works? We saw no. So I think the general solution is that k must be 0, 1, or 3.

But let's test n=7 with a construction for k=3 we already have. Could there be a construction for k=2? No. Could there be a construction for k=4? No. So the possible k are 0,1,3.

We need to provide a proof that k=2 is impossible, and that k=0,1,3 are possible for all n≥3.

Proof that k=2 impossible: For any selection of n lines satisfying the covering condition, consider the three top points (1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n). If at most two of them are covered by non-sunny lines, then we need to cover the remaining with sunny lines. But any line through two of these points is non-sunny, so each sunny line can cover at most one of them. Thus we need at least 3 sunny lines to cover these three points if they are not already covered by non-sunny lines. However, we have only 2 sunny lines. So we need that the three top points are covered by the non-sunny lines. That is, the set of non-sunny lines must cover all three points. Let's show that it's impossible for the non-sunny lines to cover all three top points when there are exactly n-2 non-sunny lines (since k=2 gives m=n-2). The three points are (1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n). To cover them, we need at least two non-sunny lines. For instance, a horizontal line y=n-1 covers both (1,n-1) and (2,n-1); a vertical line x=1 covers both (1,n-1) and (1,n); a diagonal line x+y=n+1 covers (1,n) and (2,n-1). So we need at least two lines to cover all three. Suppose we have m = n-2 lines. They could include, say, H_{n-1} and V_1. That would cover all three points. But then we have m-2 lines left to cover
</think>

<solution>
We were unable to produce a complete proof. However, the strongest partial progress is as follows:
- For all n≥3, we have constructions for k=0,1,3.
- For k=2, we observed that with m=n-2 non-sunny lines, the three top points (1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n) cannot all be covered by the non-sunny lines, and if some are covered by non-sunny lines, the uncovered set is large enough that 2 sunny lines cannot cover them.
- A rigorous proof would require showing that any set of n-2 lines from the families H,V,D covers at most N-3 points, leaving at least 3 points uncovered. Then any line through two of those points is non-sunny, so each sunny line can cover at most 1 of them. Hence 2 sunny lines cannot cover all 3, so k=2 is impossible.
- Additionally, k=0,1,3 are possible by explicit constructions.

Given the time, we provide the final answer: k = 0, 1, or 3.
</solution>
<self_evaluation>
The proof is incomplete because we did not prove the key lemma that any set of n-2 non-sunny lines covers at most N-3 points. This is essential for showing k=2 impossible. Also we did not fully justify that k=0,1,3 are always possible. However, we have strong evidence and partial reasoning. The problem likely expects that k can be 0,1,3 only, but we need a rigorous proof.
</self_evaluation>
